BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:      Federative Republic of Brazil.
Beneficiaries:    Ministry of Social Action (MAS) and State Water Companies of Santa Catarina
(CASAN), Bahia (EMBASA), and Mato Grosso do Sul (SANESUL).
Executing
Agencies:      National Secretariat for Water Supply and Sanitation (SNS), CASAN, EMBASA, and
SANESUL.
Amount:        US$250.0 million equivalent.
Terms:         Repayment in 15 years, including five years of grace, with interest at the Bank's
standard variable rate. Proceeds of the Loan will be passed on by the Federative
Republic of Brazil to CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL on the same terms and
conditions as those of the Loan, including the foreign exchange risk.
Project
Objectives:    The project's main objectives are: (i) to strengthen sector institutional capabilities
by laying the foundations of a modern and efficient legal and regulatory framework,
and upgrading the efficiency and competence of the sector's financial framework;
(ii) to help CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL to operate on a commercial basis, with
financial self-sufficiency, corporate autonomy, and accountability for their actions,
through the financing of a time-slice of ti.eir six-year investment programs; and (iii)
to improve environmental and health conditions in urban areas, through the
expansion of water supply and sewerage services.
Project
Description:   The institutional strengthening component will finance: li) the formulation of a new
regulatory farmework for Brazil's water and wastes sector, including a detailed plan
of action for institutional reformulation, drafts of proposed legislation and regulation,
and definition of functions and mechanisms at the Federal and state levels; (ii} the
evaluation and reformulation of the sector investment programming and financing
framework, aiming at upgrading its efficiency and ensuring an improvement in the
self-financing of sector companies; (iii) technical assistance and training for the SNS
and other Federal and local sector agencies to ir.crease their institutional capacity
to carry out their responsibilities under the project and under the institutional
arrangements and sector policies to be proposed; (iv) training of sector management;
and (v) preparation facilities for a proposed Water Demand Management and
Systems Rehabilitation Project.  The investil,ant component will finance the
expansion and reinforcement of the water supply and sewerage systems of CASAN,
EMBASA, and SANESUL, improve their operational efficiency, and enhance the
quality of their services.
Project
Benefits:      The project will directly benefit more than 4.3 million people with water supply and
sewerage services in hundreds of cities and towns in the states of Bahia, Mato



- vi -
Grosso do Sul, and Santa Catarina. In addition, the project will also: {i) benefit the
companies' old and new customers, through the provision ot services of bener
quality, higher reliability, lower costs, and faster access; (ii) help the companies
strengthen their financial viability, and improve their operational and management
efficiency; (iii) provide a role model for the rest of the sector; and {iv) lessen the
water sector's financial dependence on Government funding.
Project Risks:   There are no serious technical risks In the project. Possible risks are: inadequate
tariff adjustments in a continuous highly inflationary economy and the government's
willingness to continue supporting the institutional reform to strengthen the sector.
To minimize these risks: (i) agreement was reached with the Federal and local
governments to maintain current policy on tariff adjustments, which during the past
three years have produced very positive results for the financial health of the SWCs;
and Oii) a mid-term review will assess completion of all studies, degree of
implementation of proposed recommendations at Federal and state level, and
progress made by the companies on operating and financial matters.

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
1. THE WATER AND WASTES SECTOR
A. Background
1.    The accelerated urbanization that took place in Brazil during the 1 960s revealed the serious
limitations of municipalities in meeting the responsibilities allotted to them for provision of basic
sanitation services--water supply, sewerage, solid and liquid waste collection and disposal, and the
related needs for drainage and water pollution control--in an atmosphere of rapidly increasing demand.
By the end of the decade, these services were less than satisfactory in most areas, and quickly
deteriorating. Less than 50% of the urban population was connected to public water supply and less
than 25% to some form of sewerage service. Rural populations had only skeletal coverage. Untreated
effluent and uncollected solid wastes were discharged into cities' drainage systems, straining their
limited capacity, and producing clogging and flooding. This situation, which was more pronounced in
low-income urban areas, contrasted sharply with the concurrent rapid economic development taking
place in the country, and with the desire of the government to distribute the benefits of this
development to the population by improving living conditions, principally in the cities. To meet the
challenge of reducing the deficit of water supply and waste removal services, the Federal Government
established in 1971 the National Water Supply and Sanitation Program (PLANASA).
2.    PLANASA was structured under the aegis of the former National Housing Bank (BNH), as a
comprehensive program encompassing: design, financing, construction, and operation of water supply,
sewerage and sewage treatment systems; technical assistance to the state water companies (SWCs)
that were organized under the program; and training of SWC personnel. The main focus of PL*ANASA
was to help the states develop--with the establishment of the SWCs--their own financially and
technically viable organizations for the provision of water supply and sewerage services, and to provide
a stable source of financing, consisting of debt and equity from Federal and state sources. A portion
of the Federal Government Employees' Pension Fund (FGTS) was earmarked for water and sewerage
investments, and was lent to the SWCs by BNH. State resources were held in state water and
sewerage funds (FAEs), which were capitalized by a combination of state revenues and borrowing and
designed as revolving funds for state-wide sector development. Both Federal and state funds were on-
lent at interest rates which were designed with cross-subsidies from wealthier southern to poorer
northern states, and softer terms for sewage treatment than other sector investments. In 1978 a set
of clear criteria for setting tariffs was established, in the form of a National Tariff Law (NTL), which
has provided the essence of the framework for sector regulation. The NTL stipulates that operational
revenues should be sufficient to cover operating expenses and depreciation, plus a remuneration on
assets not greater than 12% but at least equal to the debt service. To maximize efficiency of
investments and operations, PLANASA policies emphasized decentralization of responsibilities from the
Federal to the state level, and institutional development of SWCs. Planning and programming at the
Federal level were the responsibility of BNH, which had state-level branch agencies and engineering
consultants working with the SWCs. Responsibility for provision of services remained with the
municipalities; most municipalities granted a concession to the SWC, which performed project
preparation, implementation, and day-to-day operations and maintenance. Municipalities representing
about 10% of the country's population decided against delegating their powers to the SWCs, and built
and currently operate their own water and sewerage systems. Municipalities and towns that chose
not to grant their concessions to SWCs were not eligible for PLANASA funds.
3.    Following the establishment of PLANASA, the water sector underwent a period of rapid
infrastructure investment and achieved impressive results. Increased urban water supply coverage was
assigned top priority within PLANASA, and the fact that the goal was largely accomplished is very



-2-
significant. PLANASA aimed to raise water supply coverage In urban areas from 45% in 1970 ts 30%
In 1985; by 1983, the actual achievement was an impressive 83%. However, these gains we.e not
matched by performance in other areas. Sewerage lagged behind, increasing from 24% in 1970 to
just 32% in 1984, as opposed to the target level of 50%. Investments in water supply, sewerage and
sewage treatment for the period 1977-1985 totaled about US$1 1 billion, of which 70% was invested
in water supply and 30% in sewerage (including a small amount for water pollution control).
PLANASA's investments during the period totalled US$9 billion, or 80% of the total, of which 66%
representeo BNH funding. In the same period, BNH financed drainage works in about 60 municipalities
amounting to about US$800 million.
4.    During the 1 980s, and particularly after 1985, the sector entered a financial and institutional
crisis that has severely affected it, leaving its agencies with large debts, and reducing the investment
rate to levels below those that can be attributed to a natural declining trend following a major
expansion. Besides Brazil's macroeconomic crisis ^nd its accompanying high inflation, the sector's
performance deteriorated due to an excessive reliance on government financing and subsidies; a lack
of appropriate asset valuation, which together with the 12% upper limit for remuneration on operating
assets substantially reduced the SWCs' margin for tariff increases; and a governmen,aI policy that
attempted to stop infiS I;on by frequently allo.,ving inadequate adjustments and instituting tariff freezes.
To cover the resulting investment financing gaps, BNH increased its share in the financing of SWCs
and later even covered oparational deficits. The resulting debt grew to suffocate the SWCs and was
eventually partially transferred to the states. This assumption of debt in turn prevented the states from
making adequate contributions to the FAEs for new investment. This financial deterioration at the
state level was accompanied by BNH's own financial problems in coping with the macroeconomic
crisis. A decision taken by the Federal Government to subsidize mortgages further eroded BNH
finances. In November, 1986, BNH was abolished and its main operational portfolios were transferred
to the Caixa Econ6mica Federal (CEF), and a unit within CEF designated to supervise water and wastes
sector investments. The selection of CEF as apex-cum-technical agency significantly weakened
PLANASA. CEF is a commercial bank which has only skeletal specialized technical suppoart, inadequate
to perform the inveystment planning and financial and operational control of the SWCs and the FAEs.
B. Water Supply and Sewerage Service Coverage
5.    Although the overali annual rate of population growth in Brazil is slowing to under three percent
(sea Annex 1, Attachment 1), urbanization rates will continue to exert pressure for expansion of basic
infrastructure. The country's total population (about 151 million), is expected to reach about 183
million by the turn of the century. Almost 76% (about 115 million) of this total is presently living in
cities, and over 80% will be urban by the year 2000. Urban rates of growth and population totals are
unevenly distributed in the country. The Southeast region has the largest urban population but the
smallest growth rate, while the Center-West region is the fastest growing in the country. The
distribution of this population has a pronounced effect on the demand for infrastructure and the way
this demand is attended will significantly affect Brazilians health. In addition, the relatively "bottom
heavy" age distribution of the population pyramid implies that household formation rates are 20%
higher than urban growth rates as a whole. This signifies an even higher demand for infrastructure
connections than would be suggested merely by urban population growth alone.  Finally, the
urbanization process is increasing the number of secondary and tertiary cities and towns. This fact
complicates the administrative burden of meeting the need for water and sanitation, given the present
emphasis on decentralization.
6.    Brazil has produced one of the most impressive records in expansion of water service of any
country in the region. During the 1 970s and 1 980s, more than 55 million people were giving access
to water and another 24 million to sanitation services with individual house connections. As a result
of this effort, present urban water and sewerage coverage levels are about 86% and 46% respectively,
as opposed to coverage levels of about 50% and 25% in 1968, respectively (see Annex 1, Attachment
3). As population totals, the levels of service coverage are also unevenly distributed in the country.



-3
The Southeast and South regions have the largest water coverage levels (92% and 88%, respectively),
while the Southeast and the Center-West regions have the la, Nest sewerage coverage levels (71% and
35%, respectively). The Northeast reglon has the lowest level of service for water supply (76%) and
the North region the lowest level of service for sewerage (7%).
C. Sector Organization
7.    The Federal Government has played an important policy-making role In the water and wastes
tector in the past. It has been responsible for dpproval of overall sector Investment levels and
priorities, and through PLANASA and BNH/CEF, has been regulating sector activities by estabMishing
tariff and cost recovery policy, credit Zerms and other normative guidelines. The states have had a
direct management role in sector operations through ownership and operation of the SWCs and
management of the FAEs. Federal and state level cooperation was maintained on a state by state level
through contract p:ans, agreed between the SWC and the state with the formal approval of BNH/CEF,
which outlined performance and monitoring targets. The decentralization reforms brought about by
the 1988 Constitution have increased the role of state and municipal governments in the organization
and regulation of the water sector. Tariff-setting, for example, has been formally transferred from the
Federa! authorities to the states--with so far very positive results for the financial health of most SWCs.
8.    The Collor administration transferred responsibility for the water and wastes sector to a
National Secretariat for Water Supply and Sanitation (SNS), within the new Ministry for Social Welfare
(MAS). The Secretariat's responsibilities include: (i) setting national sector policies for water supply
and sanitation; (ii) consolidating planning and investment priorities; (iii) coordinating sector Federal
activities with state and municipal initiatives; and (iv) supporting MAS in its function of manager of the
FGTS funds. To this end, SNS should promote, help prepare, help finance (mainly with Federal budget
and FGTS funds lend through CEF as financial intermediary), and supervise sector investment projects
and operations.
D. Sector Financing and Tariff Policy
9.    Financing sources for the sector traditionally have been based nearly fully on debt, and have
come from the earmarked FGTS, leaving sector companies saddled with large debt burdens. This
financial policy of heavy dependance on public funds and subsidies has always been unsatisfactory.
First, financing out of the FGTS funds has always been erratic because decisions are made on an
annual basis, contract by contract, and not for complete projects. And second, the companies have
not been financially motivated and made administratively capable to explore other sources of financing.
However, only during these years of strong limitations on borrowing created by the indebtedness of
most Brazilian states and the necessary fiscal constraints imposed by tl.e Federal Government, the
negative effects of this policy on the sector's financial health have become transparent. Internal cash
generation, through adequate tariff structures and levels, should become an increasing and healthy
source of investment funding.
10.   The Bank has been closely involved with the Brazilian authorities in the design of water and
sewerage tariff structures over a long period of time, including participation in the formulation of the
National Tariff Law (NTL) (see Annex 3 for an analysis of the NTL). Although a major step in the
direction of securing the financial health of the SWCs, the NTL contains some deficienci6s with regard
to the tariff structure: (i) it does not necessarily convey the right economic signals because it is based
on accounting costs; (ii) it gives inadequate guidance on the degree of cross-subsidies for the financing
of services to the poor; (iii) it does not appropriately address the issue of cost recovery for water
pollution, and other externalities; (iv) it gives inadequate guidance on the relationship between water
and sewerage charges; and (v) the suggested structure of charges to be applied to large and small
consumers is not appropriate. Also from a strictly financial point of view--i.e, in terms of defining
average rate levels, the NTL has failed to achieve its objectives--principally because of lack of
enforcement. Finally, the lack of appropriate asset valuation by the sector companies has also



4-
substantially reduced the companies' margin for tariff increases--the NTL contains a 12% upper-limit
for remuneration on net operating fixed assets.
11.   The Brazilian authorities, aware of these anomalies, organized a national workshop in April,
1988, with Bank assistance, to examine the level and structure of water supply and sewerage tariffs.
Although there has been little progress since then, the SWCs of Sgo Paulo, Parana, and Rio Grande
do Sul are presently carrying out detailed studies of their marginal cost structures, as agreed in recent
operations.
E. Investment Policy
12.   PLANASA has been the single most Important source of investment in urban areas in -the water
and wastes sector. PLANASA investment represents more than 80% of Brazil's total investment in
the sector. The remaining Investment has been made by municipalities that did not join PLANASA and
by specific programs of the Ministry of Health, principally in areas under the responsibility of the former
Fundagao de Servigos de Saude Publica. Since 1968, PLANASA has invested more than US$1 1 billion
in the sector--about 70% In water and about 30% in sanitation--benefitting more than 55 million people
In more than 4,000 communities. Almost two-thirds of these investments came from thie central
government (through CEF and BNH), nearly a quarter from the states (a part of which had been lent
to the states by BNH/CEF), and the rest from grant and outside financing.
t3.   The objective of PLANASA to rapidly and visibly address urban service deficits led to a focus
tun water supply and, to a lesser extent, sewage collection in urban areas, as the forme, were more
visible and popular among the population, and their costs more easily recovered through tariffs than
other investments. PLANASA's actions in other subsectors reflected a wavering among various
approaches. Drainage systems and solid w3ste collection and removal did not receive PLANASA
assistance and continued to be provided, with insufficient resources and poorly trained staff, by
municipalities--although a separate program under BNH later financed some limited drainage
investments in certain municipalities. Nor was the need for rural water supply and sanitation service
adequataly addressed by the several responsible agencies: rural water supply and sanitation policy
continued to be based largely on specific and often localized threats to health or on the need to
complement rural development programs.
14.   Brazil has produced one of the most impressive records in expansion of water service of any
country in the region. However, service levels have dropped perceptibly in the recent past and, merely
to maintain present levels of water (86%) and sanitation service (46%), the annual investment during
the 1990s should be about US$614 million in water supply and about US$668 million in sanitation.
The investments required for accomplishing the more ambitious but still feasible coverage targets of
95%h in water and 50% in sewerage, would require about US$676 million and US$729 million per year,
respectively. And to achieve in year 2000 the MAS/SNS' goals for 1995 (100% in water and 60%
in sewerage), the require annual investments during the decade would be about US$712 million in
water and US$875 in sewerage (see Annex 2).
15.   The limitations for investments during the 1960s made BNH and the SWCs give priority to
increasing efficiency in investment and operations. Economic selection criteria for subprojects were
introduced by BNH and water conservation programs were initiated. Despite these actions, inadequate
levels of investment led to deficiencies in existing delivery systems: more than half of the population
with access to public water systems receives less than fully satisfactory services, including some
rationing. Since investment levels will not dramatically increase in the short-term because of
macroeconomic constraints, there is a clear need for: (i) increasing investment efficiency, including
adherence to established economic, technical and financial project selection criteria; and (ii) upgrading
)f a program of operational efficiency, including a clearly defined program for reducing unaccounted-
for-water, more efficient maintenance practices, improved cost control, and additional attention to the



-5-
rehabilitation of existing networks. To address this important issue, the Project includes preparation
facilities for a proposed Water Demrand Management and Systems Rehabilitation Project (para. 43).
F. Pressing Investment Needs
Urban and Rural Poor
16.   For the past several decades  'verty in BraOi has become Increasingly an urban problem.
Although absolute numbers show tha- poor are about 47% urban and 53% rural, the rate of
growth of the urban poor has increased, vvnile that of the rural poor has declined. The issues which
are raised by the process of macroeconomic adjustment--reductions in public expenditures for
infrastructure and increases in tariffs for infrastructure services--will, cgete paribus, worsen the plight
of the poor in the short- to medium-term, as the country attempts to achieve stabilization and growth.
Since the provision of infraptructure services is linked to poverty alleviation both Indirectly (through
growth) and directly (through access to, and targeted subsidies within, the provision of such services),
additional focus during the next years needs to be placed on targeting infrastructure policy and
irvestment interventions to address tne needs of the poor.  The ongoing Water Project for
Municipalities and Low-income Areas (FY88) addresses specifically some of the issues involved In
providing water and sanitation services to Brazil's poorest families.
Water Pollution Control and Prevention
17.   Under PLANASA, with the exception of a few erratic operations, pollution control and
prevention has been neglected. Thus the increasing problem of water pollution, particularly in the
larger metropolitan centers and heavily industrial areas, has become a key sectoral issue. The Bank
is currently preparing a project whose main objective is to assist Brazil in developing a cost effective
approach to water pollution control. Cost effectiveness will require the implementation of cost
recovery mechanisms to ensure the collection of enough revenues to finance the required investment
projects. Both projects (the Water Sector Modernization and the Water Pollution Contiol) will joint
efforts towards developing a single proposal on policy and institutional issues to minimize allocati%
distortions.
G. Regulation and Enforcement
18.   Even before the abolition of BNH, PLANASA had been in need of reformulation. The sector had
moved from a situation of abundant financial resources and large "fitapped demand in congested and
wealthy urban areas, to the presert situation of constrained investment resources and demand
pr-marily in less densely populated and poor, urban areas. Another factor which urges PLANASA's
reformulation is the series of fiscal, economic and policy reforms, undertaken from 1986 to the
present, aimed at bringing the economy to a sound footing. These reforms included measures to
manage external and internal debt, and decentralize administrative and other powers to state and local
governments. Increased fiscal transfers to the states and municipalities heloed to attenuate somewhat
the hraking effects of the debt measures, but for state and municipal water companies, these
measures, although necessary from a macroeconomic point of view, constituted new constraints on
meeting investment goals. This difficult situation coincided with the financial and technical decline of
the apex institutions, BNH. and later CEF, which failed to adapt PLANASA to these changing
circumstances.
19.   In light of the demise of PLANASA, a review of the adequacy of the current regulatory and
legal framework of Brazil's water sector is timely. Specific proposals should be made regarding. Ii)
tariff policy design, in particular cost recovery of sewage treatment facilities and the extent to which
externalities, such as sewage quality and environmental damage, are accounted for in the tariff; Oiil
tariff policy enforcement. The current rate-of-return regulation should be compared with other forms
of regulation, for example, "yardstick competition"--which assigns rewards on the basis of performance



- 6 -
relative to other firms in the sector; (iii) the locus of the regulatory power. Formerly centered at the
Federal level, following the decentralization reforms, it is likely that increasing responsibilities will be
shifted to the states. For example, a Federal Government agency may be created to design and
implement broad national water policy encompassing such elements as setting national tariff policy,
mandating quality of service standards, and defining and enforcing environmental standards; while
independent expert regulatory commissions may be created to monitor and enforce license conditions,
negotiate license modifications, and control of prices and entry and exit; and (iv) financial policy for
sector companies, including availability and terms of financing for sector investments. The possibilities
of creating regional companies to provide services to a group of towns, whether public or private,
should also be explored.
H. The Polential Role of the Private Sector
20.   The basic services of water supply and sewerage are naturally monopolistic, but activities such
as sewage treatment; the construction, operation, and maintenance of water networks; metering,
billing and collection; and the operation of production facilities could be organized in more competitive
ways. Increase involvemert of the private sector may provide a better environment for delegating
some of these activities to independent operators. Feasible alternatives to operation by public
companies worth exploring are management contracts, leasing arrangements, End concession or
franchise arrangements where service rights are auctioned. In particular, state monopolization is not
the only available policy response in circumstances in which there are significant economiee associated
with monopoly supply. Therefore, full or partial privatization, where feasible and possible, should also
be considered when reviewing the adequacy of Brazil's water sector current regulatory and legal
framework.
1. The Need for a New Regulatory Framework
21.   Concerns about the efficiency and performance of the water and wastes sector have created
interest in increasing the use of commercial practices, fostering competition among service providers,
supporting decentralization, and promoting, where feasible and appropriate, greater private sector
participation. And rather than retaining an outdated regulatory framework, there is now a consensus
on the need to evaluate other options. Measures are needed to deal with recurring problems of poor
performance and to create incentives for technical, financial and managerial efficiency. Clear-cut
government policies and guidelines are considered necessary for implementing a rational and effective
regulatory framework aiming at: li) fostering competition; (ii) improving utility management; (iii)
increasing accountability; (iv) avoiding government interference in day-to-day enterprise decisions; and
(v) gaining access to new sources of capital.
22.   Desire for improved efficienc', and the recent scarcity of funds for the financing of expansion,
have increased interest in commercial methods and in attracting private capital for sector development.
However, in the absence of clear guidance as to the long-term conduct of regulatory policy, private
investors will rightly be concerned about their ability to secure an appropriate rate of return on capital
invested in the industry.  A clearly defined and stable regulatory system  is an indispensable
precondition for private capital to flow into the sector.
23.   The need to niaintain a stable and predictable industry environment calls for the separation of
the operational and regulatory activities within the sector.  Regulatory activities include the
authorization of rates that generate sufficient revenue to finance maintenance and investment and,
simultaneously, protect consumers from monopolistic pricing. A strong regulatory agency should be
protected from fluctuating political pressure and should exercise its function in an impartial and expert
fashion. An effective regulatory framework shiould be designed in accordance with the following
principles: Wi independence of the regulatory agency, (ii) transparency of the regulatory rules, (iii)
accountability in regulatory decision making, and (iv) a secure appeals procedure for the regulated
companies.



24.   In devising an effective regulatory framework for the sector, an important issue that needs to
be resolved centers on the proper state, regional, or national character of the proposed regulatory
structure. Given the movement towards administrative decentralization and the fact that the relevant
markets in the water sector are local, the most appropriate solution seem to be the creation of a
Federal regulatory commission that each state can voluntarily rely upon for regulatory oversight, and
state regulatory commissions in those states that are of sufficient size and possess the required
technical expertise.
II. SECTOR STRATEGY AND BANK EXPERIENCE
A. Government Strategy
25.   The government's strategy for the water supply and sanitation sector has been summarized
by the recently created SNS as the modernization of the sector. The modernization strategy seeks:
(i) to place the sector on a firm commercial basis, in an effort to break the familiar dilemma between
social objectives and economic rationality; (iii to increase service coverage levels, targeting in particular
the needs of the poor; (iii) to encourage a more flexible institutional organization--stimulating the
creation of municipal and regional consortia, and a larger participation and involvement of the
municipalities and the private seLtor; (iv) to increase and diversify the sector financial sources,
including a revision of issues such as the optimal mixture of project financing and cost recovery; (v)
to support an even more decentralized and efficient operation of the sector; and (vi) to place greater
emphasis on water pollution control to minimize the negative environmental impacts accumulated
during decades of little attention to the problem. To achieve the proposed strategy, SNS wants to
restructure the sector's institutional and financial framework; promote investments according to
acceptable economic, financial, technical, and social criteria--at both national and sub-national levels;
and actively support the sector's current needs of service expansion and quality enhancement. The
Government's sector goals for the period 1 990-1994 (quite ambitious given the time frame) are: (i)
water supply service to 100% of the urban population and 50% of the rural; and (ii) sewage services
to 60% of the urban population and 50% of the rural.
B. Bank Experience
The Bank's Overall Experience
26.   The majority of projects prepared in this sector have provided substantial economic and health
benefits. Since the 1960s, when the Bank began lending in this sector, the scale of projects have
become larger, with many projects involving assistance to a number of smaller entities under one
operation. Some operations have relied on intermediaries to exercise control over implementation and
to provide assistance to the beneficiary entities. And many operations have aimed at the consolidation
of water entities. The main lessons that should be learned from this experience and address in future
operations are: (i) the achievement of institutional and cost recovery objectives are painfully slow and
difficult; (ii) adequate project preparation, early land acquisition, and manageable procurement
proceduces are necessary conditions to avoid delays in project implementation; (iii) effective Bank
supervision during project implementation is a key factor towards achievement of the proposed
objectives; (iv) in strujigling for increased service levels through expansion of facilities, sound
management and operation and maintenance of completed works should receive enough attention to
avoid endangering the sustainability of the facilities and the expected benefits; (v) adequate attention
should be paid to the disposal of wastewater in densely populated areas; and (vi) greater attention
should be paid to decrease the high levels of unaccounted-for water usually observed in water
companies.



Experience In Latin America
27.   Most water and sewerage projects Implemented in the Latin America region have tr. a large
extent met their physical objectives--and in some cases have even surpassed them, but have suffered
from delays in their implementation periods--about 2 years longer than estimated during appraisal--and
failed to reach their institutional objectives. The main lessons derived from the regional experience are:
(i) water and sanitation projects are not immune to a lackluster macroeconomic performance; (ii)
frequent management changes and long implementation times compromise project objectives; liii)
physical and institutional components should be adequately linked; (iv) project scope and design should
be simple, with a minimum of cornponents; (v) projects should emphasize more cost recovery, lower
operational costs through higher operational efficiency, and a larger internal contribution to
investments; and (vi) there is a need for better demand and productivity forecast methodologies.
28.   The Project addresses rnost of these issues. Especially noteworthy are the following features:
(i) the high contributions to investment required from the companies internal cash generation (para.
89); (ii) the open competition amnong the included SWCs for Bank resources that will begin in the third
year (para. 108); (iii) the agreement reached with the beneficiaries to develop and apply improved
methodologies for planning and marginal cost pricing (para. 64 and Annexes 16 and 22); (iv) the
proposed sector institutional restructuration will considerably benefit from implementation of the
Project in the states and vice versa (paras. 39 and 40), and (v) political stability in the states is assured
during the first years of the project implementation period and for more than a half of the entire period-
-the term of office of the recently elected governors goes until March, 1995.
Experience in Brazil
29.   Since 1971, the Bank has made 13 loans amounting to US$1,370 millions for water supply
and sewerage projects in Brazil. Basic data, including the current status of each operation, are
summarized in Annex 5. Bank assistance to the sector has concentrated on supporting the objectives
of PLANASA, particularly institutional development and adoption of appropriate sector policies, and
assisting thie Government in expanding the coverage level of water supply and sanitation services. The
main project objectives were achieved in each of the eleven completed operations. In addition to
expanding the water supply and sewerage networks, these operations have helped to achieve, inter
alia: li) effective decentralization of most responsibilities to the states--SWCs were created, made
capable, and strengthened in each state; {ii) the establishment of sound economic principles for project
selection; Ciii) the enactment and application of basic tariff legislation; (iv) the establishment of a
uniform accounting system; (v) the standardization of financial, commercial and administrative
procedures; and (vi) the establishment of water conservation and rehabilitation programs and new
methods to extend services to low-income populations and rural communities. Two important lessons
from these operations are the usefulness of project mid-term reviews and of sector (as opposed to
project specifics) lending operations.
30.   The most recent experience has been dominated by the impact of the macroeconomic crisis--as
evidenced by decreasing (until recently) real tariffs, sector investment levels, and the failure of the
Federal apex institution. Efforts to adjust tariff levels to attain compliance by the SWCs with the NTL
have not been met, despite sustained efforts in these operations to reach cost recovery objectives and
financial viability of the SWCs. As noted by the Bank's Operation Evaluation Department (OED)--four
out of the eleven loans closed to date have been evaluated by it (PPARs 2929, 4613, and 7088)--, the
question of tariff levels has been perhaps the single most difficult and time-consuming issue in the
history of Bank lending to Brazil, in the water and sanitation sector as well .as in other sectors. The
lesson seems simple: to ensure economic tariff rates, future loan approvals and disbursements must
be conditioned on the meeting of existing covenants, and these should be stated in real terms or an
acceptable equivalent. In accordance with this suggestion, disbursements under the Project are subject
to a set of clear and precise company eligibility and subproject selection criteria (paras. 109 and 110),
and, to ensure the feasibility of this policy, a proposal to set-up an appropriate regulatory system will



-9-
be developed under the Project's institutional strengthening component ipara. 43). OED audit reports
also suggest that Brazilian SWCs would benefit from specialized courses in Financial Accounting under
a highly inflationary environment. The Project addresses this issue under its institutional strengthening
component, where training in this field as well as in others of special importance for the sector has
been contemplated (para. 43).
31.   The two current operations are progressing well--as of June 30, 1991, 45% of Bank loanc
under execution had been committed and 15% disbursed (see Annex 5). The Water Project in the
State of Sao Paulo of FY89 (3102-BR), a sector operation to finance a sector-wide project in Sao
Paulo--the state where the country's largest urban poor population is located, is proceeding on
schedule and achieving all proposed objectives. The Water Sector Project for Low-income Areas and
Municipalities of FY87 (2983-BR), designed to target service extension in water, sanitation, drainage,
and assistance in domestic sanitation for low-income urban residents, has suffered from extended
delays in its implementation because of constrained counterpart funds from CEF. However, presently
this project has received top priority from the new administration, and contracts were recently signed
committing the full loan amount.
32.   Ten (10) of the completed eleven (11) operations were satisfactorily executed despite the
severe macroeconomic situation confronting Brazil, reflecting the competence of management in the
Brazilian water supply and sewerage sector agencies. These completed operations achieved the main
project objectives, despite important delays attributed to inadequate institutional capabilities of some
SWCs (mostly during early stages of PLANASA implementation), failure of the states to provide timely
counterpart financing, election year grant financing of otherwise eligible subprojects in some states,
measures taken by the Federal Government to slow down public investment, and BNH (later CEF)
investment limitations. The institutional capabilities of the SWCs under the Northeast (Loan 1656-BR)
and the Multistate (Loan 1970-BR) Projects were specially weaker than those under the other Bank
operations. The abolition of BNH and the transfer of its main operational portfolios to CEF in November
21, 1986, was distinctively harmful to the sector. CEF, a commercial Bank with only skeletal
specialized technical support, has been unable to perform the investment planning and financial and
operational control of the SWCs which BNH had carried out in the past quite successfully.
33.   Ir. the Greater Sao Paulo Sewerage and Sewage Treatment Project (1525-BR), major project
components amounting to 52% of the original loan were canceled due to overestimated demand
projections and reluctance from the Federal Government to fund the works. Project investments in
sewerage treatment without additional financial returns from tariff revenues resulted in a deterioration
of the financial situation of the Water Supply and Sewerage Company of S5o Paulo. Bank experience
under this loan shows that during preparation of water pollution control projects, special emphasis
needs to be given to cost recovery mechanisms.
C. Bank Strategy
34.   The Bank's assistance strategy in Brazil is to support policies and investments that will
encourage economic growth and social development in the context of macroeconomic stability. Our
emphasis is on efficient resource allocation, increased efficiency in the public sector and the
appropriate targeting and delivery of services to the poor. In this context, our ultimate goal for the
water and sewerage sector is that operations become commercial, with any public funding explicitly
directed to provide the poor with access to appropriate services and, when justified, to protect the
environment. Poverty-targeting, finan,ial self-sufficiency and accountability of the sector agencies,
and effective pollution management are basic goals.
35.   The instruments used to achieve these objectives should include: (i) increased private sector
involvement complemented by appropriately-designed regulation; (ii) review of the adequacy of current
pricing policy for full cost recovery; (iii) targeting of subsidies to the poor; (iv) adequate investment



- 10 -
programming; Iv) public sector management efficiency through operational planning and marketing
Improvements; and (vi) environmental clean-up and prevention through appropriate regulation.
36.   Bank involvement and support of the water and wastes sector are critical at this Juncture, now
that SNS has begun strengthening its management and policy role. The Bank's lending in the sector
should help to recover and consolidate the gains it helped produce in the past. Lending should
continue to promote efficiency and to ensure the consistency of sectoral and macroeconomic policies.
111. THE PROJECT
A. Description
Background
317.   The modernization of the water supply and sanitation sector is the first priority of MAS/SNS,
as stated in its strategy for the sector". Early in 1990, this strategy and a complementary investment
program that includes this project, a National Water Pollution Control Project, a Water Demand
Management and Systems Rehabilitation Project, and an investment project based on the ongoing
Water and Sanitation Project for Low-Income Areas were presented to ald discussed with the Bank
by staff from MAS/SNS. These operations support the government's goals and objectives for the
sector and seek to consolidate the role of SNS as the sector's leading entity. In accordance with the
government's strategy (para. 25), the Water Sector Modernization Project would assist: (i) SNS in
reorienting the sector's organization and objectives to achieve higher efficiency and improved financial
viability, through decentralization, greater financial autonomy and accountability, improved tariff-setting
policies, and direct access to the financial sector; and (ii) the State Water Companies (SWCs) of Bahia
(EMBASA), Mato Grosso do Sul (SANESUL), and Santa Catarina (CASAN) in improving their standards
of commerclai performance. Two Bank missions assisted SNS and the SWCs on project preparation.
After the first preparation mission--early in 1991, SNS presented the Project's Carta Consulta to the
Departamento de Assuntos Internacionais (DEAIN). DEAIN approved the Carta Consulta on March 20,
1991. The Project was appraised from June 26 to July 19, 1991.
Objectives and Rationale
38.   The main objectives of the Project are: (i) to strengthen the sector's institutional capabilities
by laying the foundations of a modern and efficient legal and regulatory framework, and upgrading the
efficiency and competence of the sector financial model; (ii) to help CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL,
to operate on a commercial basis, with financial self-sufficiency--including independence from
earmarked funds, corporate autonomy and accountability for its actions, through the financing of a
time-slice of their six-year investment programs; and (iii) to improve environmental and health
conditions in urban areas, through the expansion of water supply and sewerage services.
39.   To strengthen the sector's institutional capabilities, the Project will address some key sectoral
issues, namely the need for: (i) clarifying Federal Government responsibility regarding the design and
implementation of broad national water policy encompassing such elements as setting national tariff
policy, mandating quality of service standards, and defining and enforcing environmental standards;
(ii) setting-up independent regulatory commissions with responsibility for monitoring and enforcing of
license conditions, negotiating license modifications, and control of prices, entry and exit, and financial
structure. To monitor their sector companies, the states would be given the option of relying upon a
Federal regulatory ccmmission or alternatively to create their own commissions or request the service
" See Minist6rio de Aq8o Social, Secretaris Nscional de Saneamento, Caixa Econ8mica Federal, Plano do A9Eo Inmediata de
Sansamento - Pat# Brasil, Srasflia, 1990.



of other states commissions; and (iii) evaluating and reformulating the sector investment planning and
financing model, including an effective decentralization of the sector's financial responsibilities.
40.   To assist CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL in improving their standards of commercial
performance, the Project will: (i) support their 1992-1997 investment programs. Efficient and
financially viable set of investments that have been carefully prepared to cover--within existing
economic and financial constraints--urgently needed extension and quality enhancernent of services
in their states, while permitting the financial and institutional strengthening of the companies; (ii) build
company capacity to mobilize investment resources internally. CASAN is expected to contribute as
a minimum counterpart funds amounting to 51 % of the planned investments or 51 % of total project
costs (54% and 55% respectively, including interest during construction) during the period 1992-1997.
EMBASA is expected to contribute as a minimum counterpart funds amounting to 52% of the planned
investments or 51 % of total project costs (54% and 55% respectively, including interest during
construction) during the period 1992-1997. SANESUL is expected to contribute as a minimum
counterpart funds amounting to 31 % of the planned investments or 51 % of total project costs (35%
and 55% respectively, including interest during construction) during the period 1992-1997; liii) help
optimize company allocation of scarce resources through better investment planning and marginal cost
analysis and pricing; (iv) further strengthen planning and programming by focusing on a realistic, rolling
six-year plan reinforced by performance agreements between the companies, the states, and SNS.
These agreements will make explicit the expectations and responsibilities of each party in carrying out
sector activities, including regulation and control, administration, investments and tariffs. Prior to Loan
Negotiations, a draft of these Letters of Agreement jointly prepared by the companies, the states, and
SNS was submitted to and found satisfactory by the Bank (see Annex 21); (v) improve company
operational efficiency and financial performance, including cost reduction. In addition, the participation
of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL--three utilities currently operating with different levels of
efficiency--will provide, to the rest of the sector, examples on how to improve corporate efficiency,
and, to SNS, a real life laboratory for testing advanced and demanding initiatives and ideas proposed
for inclusion in the new sector model, in particular, the Project's goal of strengthening sector finances
through a gradual reduction of budgetary contributions, accompanied by a substantial increase of
internally-generated funds; and (vi) improve environmental and health conditions in urban areas through
water supply service and waste water disposal.
41.   The Project forms an important part of the companies' 1992-1997 investment programs which,
at the end of its implementation period, will have benefitted about 3.3 million people with water supply
(about 1.0 million in SC, 1.7 million in BA, and 0.6 million in MS) and almost one million people with
sewerage services (about 0.6 million in SC, 0.2 million in BA, 0.2 million in MS) in hundreds of cities
and towns in these states. After 1997, a significant number of additional people will also benefit from
subprojects built with excess capacity--due to economies of scale most subprojects built to increase
water production and transmission and sewage transportation and treatment present excess capacity
during their first years of operation. In Campo Grande, for example, out of a total of 0.65 million
people that will benefit from the Project, 0.45 million people (69%) will benefit after 1997. It is
estimated that over 63% of the beneficiaries with water (55% in SC, 66% in BA, and 69% in MS) and
22% of the beneficiaries with sewerage (22% in SC, 18% in BA, and 26% in MS) are in the poverty
group.
Components
42.   The Project consists of: (i) an institutional strengthening component (2%o of project total costs)
which would finance related actions and studies focusing on the restructuring and reformulation of the
organization and objectives of the Brazilian water and wastes sector; and (ii) an investment component
(98% of project total costs) which would help finance a time-slice of the six-year investment program
of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL.



- 12 -
43.   The institutional strengthening component would provide resources for (see Annex 15): (i) the
formulation of a proposal for setting-up a new regulatory framework for Brazil's water and wastes
sector, including a detailed plan of action for institutional reformulation, drafts of proposed legislation
and regulation, and definition of functions and mechanisms at the Federal and state levels; (ii) the
evaluation and reformulation of the sector investment programming and financing model at the Federal
and local levels, aiming at upgrading its efficiency and ensuring an improvement in the self-financing
of sector companies; (iii) technical assistance and training for SNS and other Federal and local sector
agencies to increase their institutional capabilities to carry out their responsibilities under the Project
and under the institutional arrangements and sector policies to be proposed; (iv) training of sector
management in planning, marketing, and operational techniques--including cost reductions; and (v)
preparation facilities for a proposed Water Demand Management and Systems Rehabilitation Project,
under terms of reference and conditions satisfactory to the Bank.
44.   The investment component will provide resources to expand and reinforce the water supply
and sewerage systems of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL, improve their operational efficiency, and
enhance the quality of their services (see Annex 14). Overall, the companies' investment programs
are concentrated in expanding and reinforcing water distribution and sewage collection and treatment.
45.   Initially, the Bank Project would help finance about 48% of investments to be made by CASAN
in the state of Santa Catarina in the period 1992-1997 (see Annex 14, Attachment 2), about 52% of
investments to be made by EMBASA in the state of Bahia in the same period (see Annex 14,
Attachment 5), and about 60% of investments to be made by SANESUL in the state of Mato Grosso
do Sul in the same period (see Annex 14, Attachment 8). However, given that the creation of a
competitive environment between the SWCs is a key feature of this Project, the initial distribution of
resources will be guaranteed only during the first two years of project implementation. At the
beginning of the third year of project implementation and each year thereafter, the Bank and SNS will
redistribute (if necessary) all funds remaining to be disbursed, based on a revision of each company's
achievement of project objectives and compliance with agreed monitoring indicators.
46.   The Bank Project includes a large group of subprojects which become eligible for financing
under the Bank loan by complying with agreed upon economic criteria (paras. 1 10 and 1 11). Bank
projects have been carefully selected to avoid any negative consequence resulting from delays and/or
cancellations in the implementation of not Bank-financed projects (see Annex 14, Attachments 3, 6,
and 9). In addition, subprojects included in the investment plan for the project implementation period
which are not Bank-financed are also expected to comply with the same eligibility criteria applicable
to Bank projects (paras. 1 10 and 11 1).
47.   In the state of Santa Catarina, the Bank Project will help CASAN to finance: (i) the expansion
and reinforcement of the water supply systems of Florian6polis, Joinville, Criciuma, and other 12
medium-sized cities and 17 small-sized localities to improve their reliability, remove bottlenecks, and
reduce operating costs; (ii) the expansion of the sewerage systems of Florian6polis, Joinville, Criciuma,
and other 4 medium-sized cities; (iii) an operational development program addressing commercial,
financial, and operational issues in a coordinated manner to reduce unaccounted-for water levels,
increase billing and collecting, reduce operational costs, increase productivity, and institutionalize
operational planning; and (iv) future project preparation.
48.   In the state of Bahia, the Bank Project will help EMBASA to finance: (i) the expansion and
reinforcement of the water supply systems of the Metropolitan Region of Salvador, Feira de Santana,
Vitoria da Conquista, and Jequie to fully utilize existing production facilities and remove distribution
bottlenecks; (ii) the expansion of the sewerage system of the Salvador's Metropolitan Region; and (iii)
an operational development program, defined as in the State of Santa Catarina above.
49.   In the state of Mato Grosso do Sul, the Bank Project will help SANESUL to finance: (i) the
expansion and reinforcement of the water supply systems of Campo Grande, Dourados, Corumb, and



- 13 -
Tres Lagoas to remove bottlenecks, improvo their reliability and reduce their operating costs; (ii) the
cor,struction, extension and/or improvement of water production and transmission facilities in 47 small-
sized cities and towns--12 new localities will be served with water between 1992 and 1997; (iii) the
design and execution of sewerage systems in several medium-sized cities using low-cost technologies;
(iv) an operational development program, defined as in the State of Santa Catarina above; and (v)
future project preparation, including a detailed study of the state's hydro-geological potential before
December 31, 1993, and based on its conclusions and recommendations, review the economic
justification of water production subprojects involving superficial water.
The Borrower
50.   The Federal Government will be the Borrower. The Ministry of Social Welfare (MAS), through
its SNS, will supervise the Project. MAS was created in March 15, 1990, as part of the administrative
reform undertaken by the Collor Administration with the general mandate of implementing Federal
social policy. MAS comprises four Secretariats: Water Supply and Sanitation, Housing, Social
Promotion, and Civil Defense (see its organizational chart in Annex 6, Attachment 1). The creation of
the National Secretariat for Water Supply and Sanitation (SNS)--MAS' secretariat responsible for basic
sanitation policy--has finally resulted in the long overdue establishment of a Federal agency fully
dedicated to the water and wastes sector. SNS' main functions are to: (i) define national sector
policies for water supply and sanitation; (ii) consolidate planning and investment priorities; (iii) promote
and support water and wastes programs and projects aiming at increasing coverage service and quality
levels; (iv) coordinate sector federal activities with state and municipal initiatives; and (v) support MAS
in its function of manager of the FGTS funds. To this end, SNS is organized in two Departments (see
the organizational chart of SNS in Annex 6, Attachment 2): the Planning and Engineering Department
(PED) and the Programs Supervision Department (PSD). The PED is responsible for defining basic
sanitation policy directives and parameters for the allocation of resources. The PSD, responsible for
monitoring the execution of sanitation programs, has a division for urban water supply and sanitation
and other for rural services.
51.   The Bank Project will be managed by a technical unit established by Article 4 of he Borrower's
Portaria Interministerial No. 794 of December 30, 1991, and to be maintained by the Borrower for the
duration of the Project. The Project Management Unit (PMU) will be responsible for the implementation
of the institutional strengthening component (about US$8 million) and for the supervision of the
investment component (about US$492 million). The investment component will be implemented by
and under the er.tire responsibility of the beneficiaries.
52.   MAS/SNS has not implemented a Bank project before, but it is currently directing and
supervising implementation of an IDB project--the Social Emergency and Employment Generation
Project (PROSEGE). The PROSEGE loan was negotiated between MAS/SNS and the IDB late in 1990,
and has a total cost of US$500 million.
The Beneficiaries
53.   The SWCs of Santa Catarina (CASAN), Bahia (EMBASA), and Mato Grosso do Sul (SANESUL)
will be beneficiaries of and executing agencies for subprojects financed under the proposed loan,
provided they comply with eligibility and selection criteria agreed upon during Loan Negotiations (paras.
109 and 1 10). The three companies participated as beneficiaries in the Water Supply and Sewerage
Sector Project of FY83 (2249-BR). EMBASA also participated as beneficiary in the Northeast Water
Supply and Sewerage Project of FY79 (1656-BR), CASAN in the South Water Supply and Sewerage
Sector Project of rY80 (BR-1 823), and SANESUL in the Multistate Water Supply and Sewerage Project
of FY81 (1970-BR). Despite important delays, partially attributable to the severe macroeconomic
condition confronting Brazil, the companies performed satisfactorily and developed a good working
relationship with the Bank under these loans (para. 32).



- 14 -
54.   CASAN was created in 1970 with State Law No.4597 of December 31, 1970, as Companhia
Catarinense de Aguas e Saneamento; EMBASA was established in 1971 with State Law No.2929 of
May 11, 1971, as Empresa Baiana de Aguas e Saneamento S.A.; and SANESUL was created with
State Law No.71 of January 26, 1979, as Empresa de Saneamento do Mato Grosso do Sul. The
companies, limited corporations owned by their state governments, are headquartered in the state
capitals and are responsible for services in the capital city and hundreds of other localities (1177 in
CASAN, 379 in EMBASA, and 109 in SANESUL--see Annex 8).
55.   As shown in the organization charts (see Annex 6, Attachments 3, 4, and 5), each company
is governed by a Board of Directors appointed by the state governor for a period of four years. The
president of the board of directors is the Chief Executive Officer. Each of the other Directors is the
working head of one of the operational departments. The Directors are ultimately responsible to an
Administrative Council responsible for such functions as ex-ante approval of investment programs and
issuing of new shares. A Fiscal Council is responsible among other things for approving ex-post all
budgets and financial closing. This structure is common to most Brazilian state-owned companies and
is adequate to suit the companies' needs.
56.   The companies--like all SWCs in Brazil--operate under concessions granted by the municipal
governments, because water distribution is considered constitutionally delegated to the municipalities.
Under the PLANASA system, standard concession contracts were developed with a duration of 30
years and the possibility of renewal.
57.   CASAN's area of responsibility covers 178 municipalities which account for about 80% of
Santa Catarina's urban population. In its area of responsibility, CASAN serves 242 localities with
water supply and 11 with sewerage service, and has coverage levels of about 85% in water and 9%
in sewerage. EMBASA is responsible for service to 271 municipalities in the state, covering about
80% of Bahia's urban population. In its area of responsibility, EMBASA serves 379 localities with
water supply and 12 with sewerage service, and has coverage levels of about 72% in water and 9%
in sewerage. SANESUL's area of responsibility covers 65 municipalities which account for about 84%
of Mato Grosso do Sul's urban population. In its area of responsibility, SANESUL serves 109 localities
with water supply and 11 with sewerage service, and has coverage levels of about 83% in water and
11% in sewerage. (See municipalities under the companies' area of responsibility in IBRD Map 23322.)
58.   At December 31, 1990, CASAN staff numbered 3,033, EMBASA 6,096, and SANESUL 1,365.
The employees efficiency of SANESUL, as measured by the number of water and sewerage
connections per employee (235 in 1991), is one of the highest in the country. The 1991 employees
efficiency of CASAN and EMBASA (182 and 157 water and sewerage connections per employee,
respectively) is significantly lower than that of SANESUL and even lower than the 1 989 sector average
of 216 (see Annex 4). During Negotiations, agreement was reached on a gradual improvement of
these ratios (see Annex 12). In addition, programs for training, technical assistance, and managerial
skills upgrading, form a prominent part of a managerial development program included in each company
investment program (see Annex 14).
59.   Personnel costs are the most important element in the three companies structure of operating
costs. Measured as a percentage of operating costs--excluding depreciation, personnel costs are
considerable high in the three companies. During 1991, they have been estimated at about 75% in
CASAN, 66% in EMBASA, and 64% in SANESUL as compared with less than 40% in a sample of Latin
American utilities and less than 30% in a sample of Spanish and French utilities. During Negotiations,
in addition to targets for number of water and sewerage connections per employee, targets for
personnel costs as a percentage of operating costs--excluding depreciation, were also agreed to
gradually reduce personnel costs during the following years (see Annex 12).
60.   CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL use a uniform, computerized, double-entry, cost-center
accounting system developed under the PLANASA system and used by all SWCs in Brazil since 1976.



- 15 -
Major accounting procedures (reviewed in Annex 19) are consistent with internationally-accepted
accounting standards and include provisions for inflation adjustments. Monthly closings are completed
within 30 days, and the results circulated as a management report. Year-end closing are finalized
within three months of year's end. Independent external auditors review annually the companies
financial statements. These arrangements conform to the accounting and auditing guidelines of the
Bank. However, the examination of EMBASA's financial statements revealed problems In its
accounting system and procedures. As requested by the Bank as a result of these findings, prior to
Loan Negotiations EMBASA furnished a report of a reputable independent auditor on the company's
accounting systems and procedures. This report confirmed the mentioned weaknesses, and made
specific recommendations for strengthening of the company's accounting department and training of
EMBASA staff, which have been incorporated into the company's institutional development program.
61.   Customer meters are read monthly on a rotating basis by region. The companies confront
common billing and collection problems: (i) long billing periods (from meter reading to due date); and
(ii) poor accounts receivable records, due to non-payment of some users but, principally, to the
companies' historic inability to cumulatively adjust consumer bills for inflation. These circumstances
have contributed to liquidity problems (para. 90). During Negotiations, assurances were obtained from
the companies to gradually bring down the accounts receivable comparator (see Annex 12), and to
adjust overdue accounts for inflation or take other measures which would reduce the financial losses
resulting from overdue bills. Programs on billing and collection designed to improve the companies'
organizational methods, reduce billing time, and improve collection efficiency, also form part of each
company operational development program (see Annex 14). In addition, assurances were also obtained
from the states to promote and support actions aiming at improving the companies' management and
operational systems, as well as to adjust overdue accounts for inflation or take other measures which
would reduce the financial losses resulting from overdue bills.
62.   About 1983, BNH began financing programs on operational development that were highly
successful--EMBASA, for example, was able to decrease its level of unaccounted-for water to about
33%--from a 45% level, in only three years. Since 1987 however these programs have been seriously
cut in almost all SWCs due to lack of financial resources, and unaccounted-for water is again a very
serious problem. In 1990, unaccounted-for water was estimated at 44.7% in CASAN, 43.6% in
EMBASA, and 36.1% in SANESUL. As shown in Annex 4, SANESUL's figure is about the national
average (36.3%  in 1989), while CASAN's and EMBASA's are substantially higher.  During
Negotiations, assurances were obtained from the companies for gradually improving this indicator (see
Annex 12). Operational development programs, as the ones developed before, form a prominent part
of each company investment program (see Annex 14) and will be financed by the Project.
63.   Consumption metering in 1990 was 72% in CASAN, 71% in SANESUL, and 54% in EMBASA.
Although CASAN's and SANESUL's indicators are in line with the 1989 sector average (70%--see
Annex 4), these are low levels of consumption metering. EMBASA's indicator is extremely low even
for Brazilian standards. Low levels of both production and consumption metering result in unreliable
assessments of unaccounted-for water, and make cross-subsidization through tariff structures difficult.
During Negotiations, assurances were obtained to gradually increase this indicator (see Annex 12).
The operational development programs will also provide funds for this activity.
64.   PLANASA's lending system has transmitted considerable uncertainty to the SWCs' financial
planning and caused major delays in the implementation of many projects (para. 9). Brazil's economic
crisis and the transfer of sector operations to CEF has remarkably increased that uncertainty, throwing
sector planning into chaos, The companies and the states do not any longer establish priorities for the
extension of water supply and sewerage services based on assessed needs in the communities. SWCs
do not any longer use the communities' assessed needs to define service levels targets and investment
schedules, and to prepare financial projections. The Global Feasibility Study (EVG), a standardized
format used for the sector during many years with variable fortune, has became a useless tool. The
Project will address this issue by strengthening company planning and project appraising capacity--



- 16 -
badly damaged during the past years by the lack of appropriate supervision, thereby fostering the
efficient utilization of inv6stment resources within the company, Important elements of this strategy
will be the adoption of realistic, rolling six-year plans reinforced by performance agreements between
the companies and their respective states, and the use of stronger criteria for economic analysis of
subprojects.
65.   Engineering studies, design and supervision of works are executed by competent consultants.
The companies' engineering departments review designs and monitor construction. During BNH, these
departments were given advice and control by BNH's technical agents. Wlhen BNH was abolisheu and
its functions transferred to CEF, this close supervision practically disappeared. This is another area
where the Project expects to have a significant impact. The reintroduction of Technical Agents (TA)
will strengthen project programming, irnprove project implementation, and make project supervision
more efficient (para. 1 14).
B. The Economics of the Project
Demand Projection
66.   Overall water supply and sewerage service demand projections are made by the companies
separately for the markets of the capital and the interior and then added together. These projections
are supply driven. The key independent variable is the number of new connections each company
expects to hook-up to their systems in each region every year. These expected figures are then added
to the existing number of connections, convejrt-3d into number of customers and multiplied by the
volume of water per customer each company estimates to sell in each region every year. Demand is
implicitly assumed to be inelastic to price.
67.   Water supply and sewerage service demand are also independently projected by the companies
for each of the systems independently analyzed: Florian6polis, Joinville, and Criciuma in Santa
Catarina; Salvador, Feira da Santt..a, and Vitoria da Conquista in Bahia; Campo Grande, Dourados,
Corumba/Ladario, Tres Lagoas, Miranda, Sete Quedas, and Guia Lopes in Mato Grosso do Sul. These
projections are also supply driven. They have been estimated using expected population growth rates,
given targets for service coverage, estimated levels for annual per capita consumption, and declining
percentages of average losses. Demand is again implicitly assumed to be inelastic to price. (Demand
projections for Florian6polis are shown as an example in Annex 16, Attachment 1.)
68.   With this Project, CASAN, EMBASA, SANESUL will extend water supply and sewerage services
into the next century, covering additional populations of about 3.3 and 1.0 million (about 1.0 and 0.6
million people in Santa Catarina; about 1.7 and 0.2 million people in Bahia; and 0.6 and 0.2 million
people in Mato Grosso do Sul), increasing CASAN's services levels from 85% to 93% in water supply
and from 9% to 21 % in sewerage; EMBASA's services levels from 72% to 80% in water supply and
from 9% to 11 % in sewerage; and SANESUL services levels from 83% to 96% in water supply and
from 11 % to 17% in sewerage.
Investment Program
69.   The Project supports efforts by CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL to improve existing water
supply and sewerage services in their areas of responsibility (see IBRD Map 23322). The Project
emphasizes investments in water supply distribution (to complement previous production investments)
and in sewage collection and treatment. Complementary works to existing facilities will increase the
cost-benefit ratio of many subprojects, especially in water production. EMBASA, for example, will
build a power line in its new treatment plant of Pedra de Cavalho, that will allow its operation at full
capacity, the expansion of its distribution network, and a quality improvement in the areas already
served. Annex 14 contains the companies' detailed investment programs for 1992-1997.



- 17 -
70.   Service standards under the Project will consist of water supply by house connections and
sewage disposal by publiL; systems. Subprojects are being designed to conform with CEF--former BNH-
-standards. These standards are in accordance with proper management practices and the habitats,
expectations, and ability to pay of the populations benefitted. As it is customary in the Brazilian water
sector, emphasis is given in the Project to the use of low-cost sewage disposal techniques.
Consolidated service levels for each company for the project implementation period are presented in
Annex 12,
71.   Works are appropriately staged to suit the least-cost solutions. Subprojects with preliminary
feasibility studies, amounting to about 25% of total project investments and including all type of
subprojects, were assessed and found to represent the least-cost solution and to comply with the
subproject selection criteria agreed among the Bank, the SWCs, and SNS (paras. 109 and 110).
Project Cost
72.   The total cost of the Project is estimated at about US$500 million equivalent: US$8 million for
MAS/SNS component, US$161 million for the CASAN component, US$202 million for the EMBASA
component, and US$129 million for the SANESUL component. Project Costs are summarized in Table
3.1. Direct and indirect foreign exchange requirements are estimated at about US$102.4 million
(20.5% of total)--about US$4.0 million (50% of total) for the institutional strengthening component
IMAS/SNS) and about US$98.4 (20% of total) for the investment component (CASAN, EMBASA, and
SANESUL). Costs are expressed in prices of June, 1991, and include physical contingencies estimated
on average as 5% of base costs--about US$20.6 million, and price contingencies estimated on the
basis of estimated international inflation of about 3.9% per year for 1992 and thereafter--about
US$60.1 million. Due to high internal inflation rates, Project cost estimates were prepared in Cr$ and
then translated into US$. Costs include direct taxes (about US$38 million). Import duties resulting
from the recent abolition of the special exoneration enjoyed by the SWCs are not considered on the
assumption that the companies will be able to obtain a waiver of this tax. Interests during construction
are not included.
