73.   The total cost of the ProJect was determined on the basis of the companies' 1992-1997
investment programs (see Annex 14). This cost represents about 53% of the companies' investment
programs during 1992-1997 and 100% of the institutional strengthening component. During Loan
Negotiations, assurances were obtained from the companies that annual investments during the project
Implementation period will not be changed by more than 20% of the original agreed amounts, without
prior Bank approval.
74.   Costs estimates for major subprojects are based on bills of quantities and unit prices prepared
by the SWCs. Estimates for other subproJects are based on unit costs of similar works. Costs of the
institutional development programs are estimated in accordance with prevailing costs of materials and
services. About 445 man-months of consulting services are included for the institutional strengthening
component--275 man-months for the formulation of a new regulatory model for Brazil's water and
wastes sector, 50 man-months for the evaluation and reformulation of the sector Investment
programming and financing model, aiid 120 man-months for the preparation facilities for a proposed
water demand management and systems rehabilitation project. The consultants' average cost Is
estimated at about US$12,500 per man-month. This average cost represents man-month rates as
billed by consulting firms, and include salaries, social costs, and the firms' overhead and profits.
Tariff Analysis
75.   The tariff structures of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL, as presented in Annex 17, are
basically in accordance with the NTL's requirements for tariff design and levels (see Annex 3 for an
analysis of the NTL)--the only exception are EMBASA's tariff rate levels which, as indicated below
(para. 89), will need to be increase about 36% in real terms to achieve financial equilibrium.
76.   Three main characteristics--all following NTL's guidelines--can be recognized from  the
companies' tariff structures. First, tariffs are differentiated by final usage for the purpose of cross-
subsidization. Water users in industrial, commercial and public sectors face a price discrimination by
bearing rate levels ranging from about one to four times higher than those paid by residential
consumers. In SANESUL, for example, the average tariff charged to industrial water users is 3.6 times
higher than the average tariff charged to residential consumers. Figure 3.1 and 3.2 below illustrate
tariff differentiation among consumer groups and across regions. Second, all customers face minimum
consumption levels (MCLs) that translate into minimum monthly charges, which are total or partially
equivalent to the companies' fixed commercial and administrative costs of providing the service. MCLs
go from 10 m3/month--the MCL applicable to all residential consumers, to 50 m3/month--the MCL
applicable to CASAN's industrial consumers. And third, residential consumers are billed according to
a system of increasing block tariffs. The marginal tariff rates are stepwise increasing with the
consumption amount (in CASAN, for example, the tariff rate for an additional cubic meter of water
when the total amount of water consumption is between 25 m3 and 30 m3 is 60% higher than the
tariff rate when the consumption is between 15 m3 and 25 in3). As exemplified in Figure 3.3 where
SANESUL's tariff rates for residential consumers are used, this tariff schedule translates into a set of
average rates that shapes like a capital L, decreasing sharply until a minimum point is reached at a
monthly consumption of 10 m3 and then begins to increase.
77.   Sewerage tariffs are 100% of water tariffs in CASAN and 80% in SANESUL. EMBASA's
sewerage tariffs are 80% in the state capital, Salvador, 70% in the interior, and 45% in all isolated
systems (see Annex 18). These rate levels plus each company's market distribution, produce average
sewerage tariffs that are not in the same pattern as average water tariffs (see Figures 3.1 and 3.2).
In CASAN, for example, sewerage tariffs are actually higher than the corresponding water tariffs for
each consumer group. Meanwhile, average sewerage tariffs in both SANESUL and EMBASA are about
89% and 75% of average water tariffs.
78.   Average tariff levels on both water supply and sewerage collection and treatment for all
different groups of consumers are, as shown in Figure 3.1 and 3.2, significantly higher in SANESUL



- 19 -
than in CASAN, which are in turn higher than In EMBASA. Aggregated average tariff levels for
SANESUL and CASAN are closer and almost twice as high as EMBASA's tariff levels (see Annex 17).
These price differences can largely be explained by the companies' different investment financing
requirements, and by their different market structures--that Is, by the percentage of users belonging
to each tariff category.

79.   Current tariff rates are set to be progressive with respect to the amount of monthly water
consumption, conceivably to ensure satisfaction of urban residents' basic demands for water
consumption and to discourage lavish uses of the already scarce water resources. In practice, these
complexities may generate some problems. Billing and fee collection, for example, may become more
difficult and costly to manage, since consumers can take the advantage of information asymmetry to
claim themselves belonging to the category upon which a cheaper rates are charged. On the other
hand, price discrimination may stimulate adverse selectio,a problems, since some potential consumers
may find it more profitable to establish water supply facilities for themselves, e.g. digging deep wells
or set#n;,o "r 2!f-^,perated transmission pipes, hence the companies lose their opportunities to make
profits and are misled to allocate their resources to largely subsidized customers.



- 20 -
80.   Overall, resulting average wat.i rates and the combined average rates for water and sewerage
are distorted to some extent and do not reflect the companies' actual economic costs, that is, the long-
run average incremental cost (LRAIC) of providing the services. As shown in Annex 16, Attachments
5 and 6, the three companies have average non-residential rates higher and residential rates lower than
the corresponding LRAIC. Departure from marginal cost pricing is in general larger when only water
is supplied. This result, though partially explained by the difficulty of including in the calculations all
future usage of common facilities, suggests the existence of cross-subsidization favoring sewerage
users. And given that sewerage coverage is notably lower than water coverage, any subsidy favoring
sewerage is also favoring the richest groups of the population.
81.   To better serve society and meet the two basic requirements of financial self-sustainability and
social equity, the companies need to further rationalize their tariff structures, taking the following four
points into consideration. First, consumer classification shall be simplified, say, into only two
categories: residential and non-residential. It is not economically justifiable and financially profitable
to distinguish non-residential consumers and to charge them discriminated rates. Second, the miniimum
monthly charge and the progressiveness of the rates shall be carefully modified in line with the principle
of marginal cost pricing, so that cost recovery can be guaranteed while the deadweight social welfare
loss can be reduced. Third, the pattern of the tariff structures, i.e. a fixed minimum monthly charge
plus increasing block tariffs--while typical in developing countries as a means of encouraging water
conservation and implementing cross-subsidies from higher- to lower-income consumers--may not be
achieving its second desired objective. There is evidence which suggests that a significant proportion
of the cross-subsidy is enjoyed by medium- and high-income consumers, while some of the poorest
consumers are charged high average rates, because they have to pay the fixed minimum charge
afthough their consumption level is very low. And fourth, it is desirable to find a reasonable way to
correlate the tariff rates with the inflation rate, such that continuity can be assured for the companies'
long-run financial planning for investment and cost recovery.  However, before firm practical
conclusions can be drawn, costs estimates and assumptions made should be carefully reviewed to
have better estimates of the companies' marginal cost structures.
82.   During Negotiations, the companies agreed to prepare detailed tariff analyses before December
31, 1993, based on marginal costs calculated in accordance with the methodology presented in
Annexes 16 and 22, discuss the results and agree with the Bank on a set of recommendations, and
implement them before June 30, 1994.
C. The Finances of the Project
Project Financing
83.   A Bank loan amo,nting to US$250 million equivalent will help finance 100% of direct and
indirect foreign costs plus about 37% of local costs. The loan will have a term of 15 years, including
five years of grace at the Bank's standard variable intersst rate (currently 7.73%). The balance
(US$250 million) will be provided by the water companies.
84.   The financial success of the Project depends largely on the self-financing capacity of the sub-
borrowers: CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL. This feature is consequence of one of the Project's main
objectives, namely, to strengthen the companies' financial discipline and independence, making them
less reliant on government funds by requiring major cash contributions from them. This is a key
departure from practices in the past where investments were done with nearly total debt financing.
Under the Project, the companies will agree to provide a counterpart cash contribution amounting to
about 50% of the Project's total cost (54% including interest during construction). The remainder of
the companies' investment programs will be financed directly by the states. State contributions will
be madp by means of direct capital increases to the companies. Assurances were obtained from the
states that these contributions would be made available according to the Project schedule and be
totally disbursed by December, 1997.



- 21 -
85.   Table 3.2, which summarizes the Project financing plan, includes interest during construction
amounting to US$46 million. Table 3.3, which summarizes the companies' total investment financing
plans, includes interest during cjnstruction amounting to US$54.4 million. All interest during
construction will be financed by the companies. The financing plan is shown in detail in Annex 18.
During Negotiations, assurances were obtained from the SWCs to submit to tile Bank, not later than
October 15 of each year, financial projections for the following six year period, with specific details
on investment plans for the upcoming year.

Present Financial Situation
86.   The financial situation of Brazilian SWCs has been seriously affected by the country's changing
macroeconomic environment since 1983: high inflation (more than 1,000% in 1988, 1989, and 1990--
see Annex 19), periodic differentials between domestic inflation and the currency's devaluation against
the US dollar, and the quasi-annual macroeconomic adjustment plans (1986. 1987, 1989, 1990, and
1991). But besides Brazil's macroeconomic crisis, the sector's financial performance has deteriorated
due to the burden of the debt service related to investments made under the PLANASA system, an
excessive reliance on government financing and subsidies, a lack of appropriate asset valuation, and
a governnmental policy that attempted to stop inflation by frequently allowing inadequate adjustments
and instituting tariff freezes (the first tariff freeze was in 1985). Although Brazilian corporate law



- 22 -
provides for inflation accounting (National Law No.6404 of December 15, 1978), the magnitude of the
macroeconomic shocks, the variance in increases of the adjustment indexes, and lack of internal (quality
control in the companies' accounting systems, have made difficult the line-by-line comparative
interpretations of historIcal financial statements or precise conclusions regarding company profitability.
87.   The past and projected financial statements for CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL are presented
in Annex 18. Major accounting procedures, and the notes and assumptions for the forecasts, are
provided in Annex 19. A summary of financial resources transferred by the states to the companies
is presented in Annex 20. The financial performance of the companies began to improve in 1987 with
the j.e facto delegation to the state governments of the power to set tariffs. At that time, the
companiies started to readjust their tariff rates in accordance with financial recuperation plans which
had been agreed with the Bank and CEF. In 1986, the companies did not cover their operational costs,
depreciation, and financial expenses--SANESUL's operating ratio was 1.20, CASAN was 1.25, and
EMBASA was 1.30. In 1987, though EMBASA and SANESUL continued showing an operating ratio
larger than one (1.10, both of them) and compliance with the NTL was still far from being reached2'--
CASAN's compliance coefficient was 0.80, SANESUL was 0.84, and EMBASA was 0.58--a
performanice improvement was already noticeable. By 1990, the financial situation of CASAN and
SANESUL had changed considerably: their operating ratios were 0.72 and 0.79, respectively, and their
compliance ratios with the NTL were 1 .10 and 1 .13, respectively.
88.   EMBASA's overall financial situation improved only slightly due to particularly severe
constraints. Although EMBASA's tariff increases--specially those of 1 987 and 1988--were enough to
turn positive its operational result (in 1990, its operating ratio was 0.88 and its NTL compliance ratio
was 1.06), they were insufficient to provide financial health to the company because of fines and
unpaid contributions to PASEP, FINSOCIAL, INSS, and Ministerio da Educacao (Salario Educacao), as
well as fines and unpaid bills to the Companhia de Eletricidade do Estado da Bahia and unpaid taxes
(the Imposto sobre a Renda Retido na Fonte--IRRF, and the Imposto de Circulac3o de Mercadorias e
Servigos--ICMS). In addition, EMBASA was not paying its debt to CEF and FAE, and at end-1 990 this
debt was estimated at about US$193.3 million. During project preparation, the Bank asked the state
of Bahia and EMBASA to find a satisfactory solution to the company's financial debt problem, as a
condition for participating in the Project. Prior to Loan Negotiations, the state of Bahia and EMBASA
furnished to the Bank documentation demonstrating a satisfactory resolution of its overdue obligations
to government creditors. This documentation includes: (i) copies of the agreements with PASEP,
FINSOCIAL, INSS, the Ministerio da Educaggo, and the Companhia de Eletricidade do Estado da Bahia
which regularize payments over an extended amortization period; (ii) copy of the agreements which
provide for gradual repayment of overdue ICMS and IRRF obligations; (iii) copy of the agreement
between CEF, the state of Bahia and EMBASA which makes the State responsible for repaying
EMBASA's long-term debt with CEF, but leaves this debt as part of EMBASA's long-term eligibilities;
and (iv) copies of the documents that extinguishes the FAE and converts in equity EMBASA's debt to
the FAE.
89.   Tariff recovery has been notable in the three companies: SANESUL's combined water and
sewerage average tariff increased 356% from 1986 (US$ 0.16/m3) to 1990 (USS 0.73/m3), CASAN's
average tariff increased 353% from 1986 (USS 0.13/m3) to 1990 (US$ 0.59/m3), and EMBASA's
average tariff increased 247% from 1986 (US$ 0.19/m3) to 1990 (US$ 0.66/m3). Tariff recovery
however has followed different paths. While SANESUL and CASAN have been continuously increasing
their rate levels in real terms during the last four years, EMBASA concentrated its real increases in
1987 (45%) and 1988 (1 10%)--partially to minimize the effects of a reduction in its flux of external
investment resources. At appraisal time, and regardless of a new governmental tariff freeze from
February to May, 1991 --applied as part of the Collor Plan II, CASAN's tariff level were found adequate
21 The NTL compliance ratio is definer] as operating revenues over the sum of operating expenses, depreciation, and debt service.
Therefore, a water company is in compliance with the NTL when this ratio is larger than one.



- 23 -
for generating the Project's required internal cash, if kept constant in real terms. CASAN's internal
cash generation during the period 1992-1997, with current real tariff levels and achieving agreed target
levels for operational improvement (see Annex 12), would be enough to finance about 48% of total
Investment costs (52% including interest during construction). To comply with the conditionality of
the loan, SANESUL should either increase its real tariff levels or decrease its investment program in
1992 and thereafter. With real increases of 4% in 1992 and 1993, 2% in 1994 and 1995, and 1 %
in 1996 and 1997, and achieving agreed target levels for entrepreneurial operational (see Annex 12),
SANESUL's internal cash generation during the period 1992-1997 would be enough to finance about
31% of total investment costs (35% including interest during construction). To comply with the
conditionality of the loan, EMBASA should achieve average tariffs of US$0.54/m3 for water and
US$0.41 /m3 for sewerage in December, 1 991 (a real increase of about 36% in both cases), and either
apply further increases in its real tariff levels or decrease its investment program in 1993 and
thereafter. With real increases of 4% in 1993, 2% in 1994, and 1% in 1995, 1996, and 1997 (in
addition to the 36% required in 1991), and achieving agreed target levels for operational improvement
(see Annex 12), EMBASA's internal cash generation during the period 1992-1997 would be enough
to finance about 52% of total Project investment costs (54% including interest during construction).
90.   The companies' inability to make inflationary corrections on consumer bills has been partly
responsible for liquidity problems, as indicated above (para. 61). There is no monetary adjustment for
the period between billing and the due date, and policies regarding penalties for delays beyond that
date vary widely among the companies. The average collection period estimated by CASAN,
SANESUL, and EMBASA for 1991 is 58, 75, and 75 days, respectively. During Negotiations,
assurances were obtained from the companies to gradually bring down the accounts receivable
comparator (see Annex 12), and from the states to take all administrative and legal acticns necessary
to allow cumulative inflation adjustments of consumer bills.
91.   Company net fixed assets at end-1 990 are estimated to be about US$109 million (CASAN),
US$84 millions (SANESUL), and about US$197 million (EMBASA). Because of the book-value
distortions caused by inflation adjustments during the past decade (adjustment indexes do not always
move in tandem with inflation), the companies' assets are undervalued. A recent study in the SWC
of Sao Paulo, for example, recommended to multiply by 4.6 the net assets of the Metropolitan Region
of Sao Paulo. During Negotiations assurances were obtained from each company to undertake a study
on the amounts involved and likely fiscal implications of an asset revaluation before June 30, 1 993.
The study should be based on current replacement costs and useful asset life and the
recommendations should be incorporated into the end-year 1 993 financial statements. In addition,
each company was requested to undertake a subsequent study every three years to determine the
necessity of further physical revaluations. Because of off-setting equity entries, this step should
improve the companies' capital structures.
Projected Financial Performance
92.   Financial forecasts for the ,,eriod 1992-1997 (see Annex 12) are based on conservative
assumptions: (i) financing plans only include expected loans from CEF equivalent to the contracts
already signed and, as is now current practice, resources from the FAEs equivalent only to forthcoming
amortization; (ii) the annual average of projected grants from the states are below the observed 1 986-
1990 annual average (see Annex 20); (iii) investment programs are as high as the companies expected
financial results allow them to be; (iv) with EMBASA's exception, required tariff levels are very close
to their current levels (para. 89); and (v) the expected increases in productivity and efficiency, though
important, are reasonable and feasible to reach (see Annex 12).
93.   To further strengthen the financial condition of the companies, assurances were obtained from
them during Negotiations: li) to generate sufficient internal cash to cover annually an increasing
percentage of their total investment programs: 20% in 1992, 22% in 1993, 24% in 1994, 27% in



- 24 -
1995, 31 % in 1996, and 35% in 1997 and every year thereafter3'. However, given that the current
stage of the budgetary process of the States and municipalities, which includes extraordinary
contributions to the SWCs, will result in a much higher level of State and Municipal financed
Investments for 1 992 that the one envisaged during Appraisal, it was agreed during Loan Negotiations
that solely for CY92 the total investment of the companies for such year will not include an amount
equivalent to US$30 millions for EMBASA, US$15 millions for SANESUL, and US$18 millions for
CASAN for purposes of the calculation of the percentage of internal cash generation to investments
durinig such year; {ii) to comply with the requirements of the NTL (i.e., to cover all operating expenses,
depreciation charges, and debt service costs);  liii) to revalue their fixed assets to obtain real
investment remunerations; (iv) to gradually increase the number of water and sewerage connections
per employee; Iv) to gradually decrease personnel cost as a percentage of operating costs; (vi) to
achieve a debt service coverage ratio no lower than 1.5; and (vii) to carry out action plans aimed at
improving their commercial performance, including the specific set of operational and financial
performance targets presented in Annex 12. In sddition, assurances were also obtained from the
States: (i) to set water and sewerage tariffs rates at levels that allow the companies to comply with
the NTL and to achieve the generation of adequate internal cash consistent with the Project's financial
plans; and (ii) to promote and support any action that would imply an improvement of the companies'
management and operational systems.
Financial Transfers of the States
94.   The states of Santa Catarina, Bahia, and Mato Grosso do Sul--as any other state in Brazil--have
transferred important amounts of money to the SWCs, since its creation during the 1970s. These
transfers are partially made to compensate the SWCs for the amounts of money that they give away--
complying with the states' political decision--to many poor customers through highly subsidized tariffs
(see Annexes 16 and 17), and partially to ensure the financial solvency of the companies--the states
are the companies' major shareholders. These transfers have been made either directly to the SWCs--
to increase their working capital and/or make total or partial payments of their debt service, or
indirectly as contributions of counterpart funds to the FAEs. Most of these contributions have been
made as grants--even the money received from the FAEs as loans have usually ended-up effectively
as grants because new loans are used to cancel out the debt service payments. During the period
1986-1990, these transfers to the SWCs have averaged: US$4.8 million per year (0.7% of totai state
revenues) in Santa Catarina, US$7.7 million per year (6.4% of total state revenues) in Mato Grosso
do Sul, and US$19.9 million per year (2.0% of total state revenues) in Bahia. Contributions during the
period 1992-1997 are forecast to be below 1986-1990 values and average: US$4.2 million per year
in Santa Catarina, US$5.2 million per year in Mato Grosso do Sul, and US$14.1 million per year in
Bahia.
D. Social and Environmental Impacts
Urban Poverty Impact
95.   The urban poor will comprise about 54% of the beneficiaries of this Project--about 2.31 million
people. Urban poor are defined in Brazil as families earning a monetary income of three minimum
salaries or less. In Santa Catarina, CASAN estimates that 44% of the benefitted population (about
0.66 million people during the life of the loan) will be urban poor. In Bahia, EMBASA estimates that
60% of the benefitted population (about 1.1 7 million people during the life of the loan) will be urban
3' Given the companies' investment profile and the expected improvements in their operational and financial performance, the
companies' required contribution of internal cash generation to investment indicators contained in Annex 12 depert significantly
from the agreed targets. These differences are particularly notable during the last years of the planning horizon when
investments tend to be underestimated.



- 25 -
poor. In Mato Grosso do Sul, SANESUL estimates that 60% of the benef;tted population (about 0.48
million people during the life of the loan) will be urban poor.
Affordability
96.   CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL apply a minimum consumption level IMCL) of 10 cubic meter
per month to their residential consumers. The MCL, which is intended to provide households with
enough water to satisfy basic needs and maintain minimum health and sanitar-, conditions, is charged
a minimum monthly charge (MMC). The MMC for water consumption for residential consumers is
about 4.6% of one minimum salary (MS)--Cr$23, 132 as of June, 1991 --in CASAN, about 3.2% of one
MS in EMBASA, and about 5.2% of one MS in SANESUL. The combined MMC for both water and
sewerage services for residential consumers is about 9.2% of one MS in CASAN, varies from 4.6%
to 5.7% of one MS in EMBASA, and is about 8.8% of one MS in SANESUL. These MMC are well
within the range of acceptability by Bank standards. (A standard often accepted in Bank projects has
been to keep water and sanitation charges under 5% of three minimum salaries.) In addition, CASAN
and EMBASA have a special tariff category for their poorest residential consumners, which is very
affordable even for the poorest segment of the population. In CASAN, the special tariff rates--about
33% of the ordinary residential tariff rates--are applied to about 2% of the residential consumers. In
EMBASA, these special rates are larger on percentage terms--about 59% of the ordinary residential
rates, but are applied to a largest segment of the residential consumers--about 26%.
Environmental Impact
97.   The Project will significantly improve sanitary and environmental conditions in the area of
responsibility of the SWCs, and no major environmental problems are envisaged. The Project will help
finance sewage collection and treatment in greater proportions than in past Bank projects--about 34%
of total investment will go to sewerage. Besides major facilities to be built in the capitals and other
large cities, the Project also includes low cost sanitation programs to be implemented on the
countryside and metropolitan peripheral areas, where conventional systems would not be financially
viable. CASAN's investment program includes almost the same amount of financial resources for
sewerage as for water supply systems (see Annex 14, Section A), increasing the company's sewerage
coverage level from the current 9% to 21% in 1997. All of CASAN's sewerage projects will be
designed and built including final treatment and disposal. As a result of this policy, many touristic
areas of the state seashore will turn to be appropriate for bathing. EMBASA's investment program is
concentrated in the Metropolitan Region of Salvador and includes several facilities aiming at improving
the sanitary conditions of the city and the quality of the seashore (see Annex 14, Section B).
SANESUL's investment program includes the construction of a complete sewerage system in Campo
Grande and important facilities in several medium- and small-sized localities (see Annex 14, Section
C). The facilities to be built in Campo Grande will reduce the organic pollution on the Prosa and
Segredo Rivers and protect the riverside community from the risk of contamination during rainy season
flooding.
98.   Several ways to improve environmental conditions in the states were discussed with the
companies and agreement reached on the following: {ii to ensure that each subproject is
ernvironmentally sound and sustainable, all subprojects will be properly screened by the companies and
subsequently by the technical agents to determine the degree, if any, to which potential environmental
impacts may occur, as well as the necessary measures to prevent or mitigate any adverse effects; (ii)
to keep the concentration level of chlorine above 0.1 mg per liter of treated water in all their water
distribution systems. A plan to monitor compliance with this agreement--based on as many sampling
points as necessary to give a good statistical representation, will be presented by the companies and
approved by the Bank during negotiations; {ii) to take all necessary provisions to assure an efficient
operation of sewage treatment plants in all major cities; and (iii) CASAN and EMBASA in particular, to
develop a plan to monitor bacteria contamination on the metropolitan bay areas that will benefitted
from the Project, to recover them for citizen use according to the Federal standard for ocean bathing.



- 26 -
In addition, the state governments will review current information collection systems on diarrheal
disease incidence, to detect improvements resulting from the expansion of water supply and sanitation
systems.  The operational indicators contained in these agreements will be monitored during
supervision and carefully evaluated at the mid-term review (para. 120).
99.   To assess tho Projects' environmental impact, the companies are required by Federal Law
(Resolution 001, 23 January 1986) to prepare environmental impact reports (RIMAs) following state
guidelines. During Appraisal, the capacity to assess the RIMAS was reviewed and found sufficient in
the state of Santa Catarina, but not completely satisfactory in the sates of Bahia and Mato Grosso do
Sul. Santa Catarina's environmental agency is a nationally well known control agency that has about
200 employees spread all over the state at 8 regional offices and runs 5 state parks. The agency has
good experience on managing environmental control assessments--including many difficult cases, and
has succeed on cleaning up some important rivers. Bahia's environmental agency needs to be
strengthened to catch up with an increasing demand from society's growing ecological awareness.
The agency's institutional capacity to control environmental degradation is very incipient. The agency
has no experience introducing sanitation projects on the permit system and, so far, Bahia's
environmental control system has not issued any license to EMBASA. Mato Grosso do Sul's
environmental agency has been trying to introduce environmental impact assessments as a routine tool
and has been training many members of its staff at these techniques, but its institutional capacity to
control environmental degradation is still incipient. The agency needs more experience, training, and
institutional strengthening. During Negotiations, it was agreed that SNS will set up a program to
provide training and institutional strengthening to the environmental agencies of Bahia and Mato Grosso
do Sul, in particular on water and wastes related issues.
E. Economic and Financial Benefits
100.  The Project will directly benefit more than 4.3 million people with water supply and sewerage
services In hundreds of cities and towns in the states of Bahia, Mato Grosso do Sul, and Santa
Catarina. The Project will also generate significant additional financial and economic benefits to water
consumers, the three companies, and the Federal and state governments.
101.  Among the economic benefits the Project brings to the consumers, in addition to the expansion
of coverage service levels, the following deserve special mention: (i) all customers of the three
companies, old and new, will be provided services of better quality, higher reliability, faster access and
lower costs; (ii) the incremental consumer surplus due to the Project is substantial because of the
increase of aggregate water consumption and the better condition of the water supplied, which may
increase people's "willingness to pay"; (iii) benefits from improvement of sanitation conditions, such
as pollution abatement, health amelioration and refinement of the living environment, are also
significant; and (iv) the consumers can furthermore gain from the benign side-effects of the Project,
such as better employment opportunities and more advantageous positions in the market of consumer
goods because of the development of other sectors pushed forward by the modernization of the water
sector.
102.  Besides the financial profitability required to recover the investment cost, the Project will
directly help CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL to: li) strengthen financial viability, through better
investment and financial planning and pricing; (ii) improve nianagement efficiency; (iii) reduce
unaccounted-for water levels, (iv) enlarge infrastructure capacities, (v) restore water supply reliability,
and (vi) reduce operating costs. In addition, the Project will also indirectly benefit remaining Brazilian
water companies by providing them with a role model to follow and by strengthening sector
institutional capabilities.
103.  And besides lessening the water sector's financial dependence on government funding, the
Project will also benefit the Federal and state governments by: (i) creating employment opportunities
in both the Project and other enterprises made feasible from the enlargement and improvement of



- 27 -
water supply and sewerage collection and treatment; {ii} increasing the gross domestic products in both
the water sector and other related sectors; (iii) expanding the tax bases; (iv) reducing government
expenditure on social welfare programs, such as public health improvement and environmental
protection; and (v) other revenues generated from the forward and backward effects of the Project,
such as providing inputs for water intensive industries or generating larger demanGs for industries that
provide inputs to the water sector.
104.  The quantification of the economic benefits generated by the Project is difficult due to: li) non-
quantifiable nature of some benefits, notably better public health and an improved quality of life; and
tii) lack of adequate market data and relevant information, such as price elasticities of demand and
reliable conversion factors for the water sector. Therefore, following a common practice in appraising
water and sanitation projects in the Bank and with a sound understanding that the Project's economic
benefits are certainly higher, the iinancial benefits generated by the Project are used to determine its
feasibility. The companies' net incremental revenues have been used as a proxy of the Project's
financial benefits, again on the understanding that they provide a minimum measure of the Project's
economic benefits. The financial rates of return (FRR) calculated using there cash flows should be also
understood as a lower bound of the Project's economic viability.
105.  Financial rates of return (FRRs) have been calculated for water and sewerage as a joint service,
since in many casus consumers pay a combined bill for both. Although separate charges are provided
in the tariff structures for water and sewerage (see Annex 17), it is not possible to identify the
"willingness-to-pay" for each service separately. All investment and operating costs and revenues are
expressed in constant market prices (US$ as of June, 1991 M--investment costs include all
complementary investments necessary to achieve the full benefits of the Project. A project life of 30
years has been used for all FRR calculations.
106.  The FRRs calculated using current tariffs for water projects are 36%  for Florianapolis'
Integrated System (CASAN), 17% for Salvador Metropolitan Region (EMBASA), and 14% for Campo
Grande, Corumbd/Ladario, Tres Lagoas, Miranda, Guia Lopes, and Sete Quedas (SANESUL). And the
FRR calculated using current tariffs for Florian6polis sewerage system is 12%. The FRR for the entire
Project is estimated as about 17%, using these systems as sample of the companies' 1992-1997
investment programs. These systems represent about one third of these programs, and localities of
every size. These FRRs compare highly favorably with the 10% opportunity cost of capital (OCC)
utilized in the analysis, and are moderately sensitive to increases in costs or decreases in revenues.
For example, an increase of 10% in investment and operating costs combined with a 10% decrease
in revenues reduces the FRRs to only 27% in Florian6polis (8% in its sewerage system), 13% in
Salvador, 10% in the six localities of SANESUL, and 13% in the entire system. Proposed tariff
adjustments in EMBASA and SANESUL (para. 89) would remarkably increase the FRR of Salvador (from
17% to 29%), but would only slightly increase the FRR of the six localities of SANESUL (from 13%
to 17%)--because of EMBASA's weight, the FRR of the whole system would also have a noticeable
Increase (from about 17% to about 23%).
F. Risk Assessment
107.  There are no serious technical risks in the Project. Possible risks include: (i) inadequate tariff
adjustments in a continuing highly inflationary economy; (ii) the government's willingness to continue
supporting the institutional reform to strengthen the sector; and (iii) the institutional capability of
MAS/SNS. To minimize these risks: {i) agreement will be reached with the Federal and local
governments to maintain current policy on tar-iff adjustments, which during the past three years have
produced very positive results for the financial health of the SWCs; {ii} a mid-term review will assess
completion of all studies, degree of implementation of proposed recommendations at Federal and state
level, and progress made by the companies on operating and financial matters; liii) a PMU in SNS,
supported by consultants, will assure an efficient technical and administrative direction of the Project;



- 28 -
and (iv) the investment component has been designed to be executed with total independence from
Federal funding.
IV. PROJECT EXECUTION
A. Company Eligibility
108.  CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL will be eligible for new subproject financing only if they
comply with the following conditions: (i) submission to the Bank before November 30 of each year,
of an updated investment program for the following six-year period--including a detailed investment
plan for the upcoming year, and updated general financial projections for the same period; (ii)
compliance with the NTL; (iii) demonstration of each company's solvency, profitability, and liquidity
according to standard financial ratios (see Annex 12); (iv) generation of enough internal cash to
annually cover a gradually larger percentage of new investment, beginning with at least 20% in year
1992, reaching at least 24% by 1994, and at least 35% by the last year of the Project; and (v)
compliance with agreed targets for number of water and sewerage connections per employee and
personnel costs as a percentage of operating costs--excluding depreciation. The TA will verify
company eligibility for disbursements, prior to assess compliance with agreed technical criteria for
subproject selection (paras. 110 and 111 ). The Bank will guarantee the proposed minimum levels of
financing (Table 3.2) only during the first two years of project execution or until March 31, 1994,
whichever is first. After this period, Bank funds will be freely allocated to eligible subprojects provided
that the companies meet technical and financial capability criteria.
B. Subproject Selection Criteria
109.  Each proposed subproject should correspond to the least-cost solution, and should be the
object of a feasibility study prepared in accordance with the guidelines for evaluation of subprojects
presented in Annex 22. All feasibility studies should be prepared by the SWC, reviewed by the
technical agent (TA), and approved by the PMU. The analysis of a given subproject seeks to establish
the priority of the proposed components, its consistency with sector policies, and its contribution to
improve the delivery of services in a given locality. This analysis also aims to ascertain that neither
the subproject nor its components adversely affect the environment and that their benefits equal or
exceed their costs. The justification of each individual subproject will be based on the long-run average
incremental costs (LRAIC) vis-a-vis the company's current average tariffs. LRAIC will be calculated
on the basis of the incremental demand it will satisfy, the estimated investment cost--including
complementary works, and the estimated operational and maintenance cost (see Annex 16).
110.  In large cities--more than 500 thousand inhabitants, water projects will be approved only if the
company's current average water tariff in that city (AWTi) exceeds 75% of the marginal cost of
providing the water supply service to residential customers with the given project (MCWi) in 1992,
80% in 1993, and 85% in 1994 and thereafter. In medium cities--more than 50 thousand inhabitants
but less or equal than 500 thousand, water projects will be approved only if the AWTi exceeds 75%
of the MCWi in 1992 and 80% in 1993 and thereafter. In small cities and towns--more than 5
thousand inhabitants but less or equal than 50 thousand, water projects will be approved only if the
AWTi exceeds 60% of the MCWi in 1992, 65% in 1993, and 70% in 1994 and thereafter. In rural
communities--population less or equal than 5 thousand, water projects will be approved only if the
AWTi exceeds 60% of the MCWi. Sewerage projects in large cities will be approved only if the
company's current average sewerage tariff in the project's site (ASTi) exceeds 60% of the marginal
cost of providing the sewerage service to residential customers with the given project (MCSi) in 1992,
65% in 1993, 75% in 1994, 80% in 1995, and 85% in 1996 and thereafter. Sewerage projects in
medium cities will be approved only if the ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi in 1992, 65% in 1993,
70% in 1994, 75% in 1995, and 80% in 1996 and thereafter. Sewerage projects in small cities and
towns will be approved only if the ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi in 1992 and 1993, 65% in 1994



- 29 -
and 1995, and 70% in 1996 and thereafter. Sewerage projects in rural communities will be approved
only if the ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi.
111.  Projects not satisfying the above conditions but which are designed for minimum cost may be
accepted by the Bank on a case-by-case basis. The analysis should explicitly address the following
issues: (i) the lack of alternative and feasible solutions; (ii) the impossibility of recovering costs through
tariffs; and (iii) the impact on poverty alleviation and health conditions. These subprojects are likely
to be in localities where urban poverty prevails and large investments are needed to compensate for
the lack of previous investments.
112.  Assurances were obtained during Loan Negotiations that: (i) subprojects should meet project
selection criteria as a condition of disbursement for the specific works; and (ii) selection and design
of subprojects outside the Bank-financed part of the companies' investment program should be based
on the same eligibility criteria agreed for subprojects under the Loan. During the course of project
execution, amendments to selection criteria may be made as a result of new marginal cost calculations.
C. Implementation Arrangements
113.  The Project, estimated to last from 1992 to 1997, will be managed by the Project Management
Unit (PMU), a technical unit established jointly by the MAS and the MEFP for the duration of the
Project (para 51). The PMU, headed by a Technical Coordinator supported by a small staff of full-time
employees, will be specifically responsible for the implementation of the institutional strengthening
component, for the coordination and supervision of the Technical Agents (TAs) to be selected to
supervise implementation of the investment component, and for the coordination of activities necessary
to translate the resulting set of proposals into appropriate legislation. The PMU will hire and retain
teams of consultants to carry out most of the studies and training comprised in the institutional
strengthening component. At Negotiations, agreement was obtained from the Federal Government on
terms of reference for the creation and staffing of the PMU, as well as on the qualifications and
experience of the unit manager, satisfactory to the Bank. CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL will be
entirely responsible for implementation of the investment component. The contractual documents will
be as follows: (i) a Loan Agreement between the Bank and the Borrower; (ii) Project Agreements
between the Bank and each company; and (iii) Shareholder Agreements between the Bank and each
state government.
114.  The TAs will report to and work under terms of reference defined by SNS/PMU, but will be paid
by the DTN--through a commercial bank, on behalf of the companies and making use of Bank
resources. The Borrower wiUl sign an agreement to that effect with each company, and not later than
September 30, 1992, should have signed Contracts of Consulting Services with the selected TAs. The
TAs will have the following functions: (i) before March 31 of each year, verify company compliance
with eligibility criteria for subproject financing; (ii) before March 31 of each year, submit an annual
report to the Bank and SNS, through PMU, on the companies' compliance with eligibility criteria for
subproject financing, (iii) after receiving each subproject or package of subprojects from a company
eligible for financing, verify compliance of each subproject with agreed selection criteria and, if
positive, review engineering designs; (iv) inform the Departamento do Tesouro Nacional (DTN) of
subprojects ready to be built with project financing; (v) submit quarterly reports to the Bank and SNS,
through PMU, summarizing subprojects compliance or not with agreed selection criteria; (vi) supervise
project execution, including inspection and expediting, certification of invoices submitted by
contractors and suppliers, and technical services connected with the interpretation of contract
documents; (vii) supervise financial performance and help the DTN to audit financial expenses; (viii)
review procurement documents to verify compliance with Bank procedures and methods; (ix) twice
a year, at March 31 and September 30 of each year, verify companies' compliance with performance
indicators defined in Letters of Agreement accorded with the Federal and state governments; and (x)
submit annual reports to SNS, through PMU, on project execution, and its main lessons and



- 30 -
experiences. During Negotiations, agreement was obtained from the Federal Government on TOR for
the selection of TAs satisfactory to the Bank.
115.  Bank resources will flow to the companies as follows (see Annex 7): (i) DTN signs a Carta
Reversal with a financial institution acceptable to the Bank--Banco do Brasil, for example, to arrange
the transfer of Bank funds; lii) MAS includes in its budget resources in favor of the Ministry of
Economy, Finance, and Planning (MEFP); (iii) from their own resources and through a commercial bank,
DTN disburses funds for subprojects cleared by the TA; (iv) the commercial bank signs Contracts of
Financing with each company and then advances to them the funds received from DTN; (v) the
companies submit to DTN supporting documentation and evidence of payments made with external
funds and corresponding internal counterpart; and Cvi) DTN withdraws equivalent amounts from the
project's Special Account. Prior to Loan Negotiations, the states and the companies furnished financial
counter-guarantees to DTN, including legal authorizations from the State Assemblies.
116. Repayment of Bank resources will be made as follows: li) the companies pay back to the
commercial bank interest and amortization resulting from the Bank loan, in accordance with the same
terms and conditions as those of the Loan (para. 83), including the foreign exchange risk and a service
charge of 0.2% (1/5 of one percent) to cover administrative costs; (ii) the commercial bank transfers
these resources to DTN; (iii) DTN assimilates these resources as Federal Government revenues; and
Civ) in the dates established in the Loan Agreement, DTN repays the loan to the Bank making use of
the resources previously included in MAS' budget and withdrawing the money from the account of the
Federal Government where the resources were deposited. The financial risk associated with delays
in or lack of payment will be borne by the Federal Government.
117.  Annual project investments are presented in Annex 14. These investment tables will be
updated semi-annually and, together with the financial projections (see Annex 18), the monitoring
indicators (see Annex 12), the status of the advancement of physical works, and the Letters of
Agreement (see Annex 21), form the basis for semi-annual progress reports. The investment schedules
are compatible with the executing capabilities of each SWC. The Project is expected to be completed
by June 30, 1997. A final agreement was reached during Loan Negotiations on annual investment
figures, monitoring indicators, and the submission to the Bank of progress reports on a quarterly basis
(para. 121). To avoid delays in project implementation, assurances were obtained during Loan
Negotiations that all funds will be on-lent to the companies as envisaged in the financial plan of the
Project (see Annex 1 8). Prior to Loan Effectiveness, Letters of Agreement among the SWCs, the state
governments, and SNS satisfactory to the Bank, should be signed and submitted to the Bank. Two
workshops will be held for purposes of orientation and explanation of Bank procedures: the first not
later than two months after Loan Effectivenes; and the second by mid-project implementation but
before December 31, 1994.
D. Project Supervision
118.  As originally structured, the PLANASA system provided a network of qualified technical staff
in BNH who were capable of fulfilling a large part of the supervision function for Bank financed
projects. In particular, with the sector loans which involved many states, BNH's state-based staff and
the technical consultants hired by BNH were rolied upon by the Bank for major contributions to the
supervision effort. However, since BNH was abolished and its opera.ional portfolios transferred to CEF,
the role of the water and wastes sector financial intermediary has been changing from one which
provided strong technical support capabilities to a role solely of financial intermediation. As an answer
to this new reality, the Project reintroduces the use of technical consultants--the TA--as the agents in
charge of supervision. Supervision activities of the TAs will include inter-alia the review of company
compliance with agreed eligibility and subproject selection criteria--including engineering studies of
subprojects, procurement, and disbursement (para. 114).



- 31 -
119.  The Project will require more than customary supervision by the Bank, to assure its successful
implementation. Extensive project reviews, to be held each year, will focus on the execution of the
institutional strengthening component, compliance with procurement issues, and the companies'
financial performance and compliance with monitoring indicators. To ensure the timely acquisition of
goods and works and contracting of consultants' services, special attention will be given to
procurement issues. EMBASA, CASAN, and SANESUL have executed Bank-financed projects under
sector loans made to, and directly supervised by. either BNH or CEF, but have never carried out a
Bank-financed project under the direct supervision of the Bank. Therefore, in addition to the usual
review of procurement documents and decisions, Bank supervision activities will include guidance in
procurement planning and on drafting of procurement documents, in particular during the first year of
project execution. Before Loan Negotiations, standard procurement documents jointly prepared by the
companies were submitted to the Bank. This strategy will significantly reduce the risk of having major
procurement problems.
120.  A mid-term project review, tentatively scheduled for June, 1994, will: (i) assess completion
of all studies included in the institutional strengthening component, as well as degree of
implementation of proposed recommendations at Federal and state level; (ii) evaluate progress made
by the SWCs in carrying out plans to improve their commercial performance, as measured by their
degree of compliance with agreed operating and financial monitoring indicators (see Annex 12); (iii)
assess the effectiveness of the training and institutional strengthening to be provided by SNS to the
environmental agencies of Bahia and Mato Grosso do Sul; and (iv) verify compliance with covenants
of the Loan and Project Agreements. During the mid-term review, particular importance will be given
to advancements made in the institutional restructuration of the water and wastes sector and to
improvements achieved in the financial performance of the SWCs. During Negotiations, agreement
was reached with the Federal Government, the states, and the SWCs, to hold a mid-term project
revipw not later than June 30, 1994.
E. Reporting
121.  Progress reports, based on timely and efficient project supervision by the TAs, will describe and
compare past achievements to appraisal projections, review the compliance with the monitoring
indicators detailed in Annex 12, and provide a critical assessment of problems and issues arising during
project execution. They will also propose adjustments and remedial actions in case of unsatisfactory
progress. The format of progress reports will be developed jointly by the Bank, PMU, and the
companies after Loan Signature. During Loan Negotiations, agreement was reached with the Borrower
to ensure that: (i) complete progress reports will be submitted to the Bank by April 15 and October 15
of each year. These complete progress reports will include an assessment of the advancement of the
physical works under execution, an analysis of the companies' operational and financial performance
vis-a-vis the agreed monitoring indicators (Annex 12), and general financial projections and updated
investment plans for the following six-year period; (ii) progress reports including only an assessment
of the advancement of the physical works under execution will be submitted to the Bank by January
15 and July 1 5 of each year; and (iii) a final report analyzing the Project's key issues and containing
a retrospective assessment of project implementation from the Borrower's own perspective, will be
submitted to the Bank not later than six months after the loan closing date.
F. Monitoring Indicators
122.  A set of operational and financial monitoring indicators developed during appraisal (see Annex
12) will constitute the basis for project reviews and supervision, including the mid-term review (para.
120). A condition for company eligibility is compliance with five of those indicators, namely: {i)
number of water and sewerage connections per employee; (ii) personnel costs as a percentage of
operating costs--excluding depreciation; (iii) compliance with the NTL; (iv) debt service coverage; and
{v) internal contribution to investment (para. 108). The companies' performance will be judged
comparing their actual results with the entire set of agreed monitoring indicators presented in Annex



- 32 -
12. In addition to the indicators listed above, particular importance will be attached during supervision
and project reviews to the unaccounted-for-water percentage, the accounts receivable comparator, and
the percentage of water connections with meters. The entire set of monitoring indicators are
contained in Supplemental Letters to the Project Agreements between the Bank and the companies,
as wel! as in the Letters of Agreement to be signed among SNS, the states, and the companies.
G. Procurement
123.  Civil works would represent about 46.5% of the total project cost, equipment and materials
about 46.5%, and engineering and technical assistance--including consulting services under the
institutional strengthening component--the remaining 7%. All contracts which can be packaged to
exceed the equivalent of US$5 million for civil works and US$250 thousand for equipment or materials
will be procured on the basis of international competitive bidding (ICB) following the Bank's guidelines
for procurement. Domestic manufacturers will be guaranteed a margin of preference under ICB
contracts for goods, in accordance with Bank Guidelines. All other contracts for civil works and
equipment or materials not exceeding aggregate amounts of about US$139.3 million and US$46.4
million, respectively, will be procured under nationally advertised local competitive bidding (LCB)
procedures acceptable to the Bank. Under these assumptions, 60% of works and materials will be
procured under ICB. The nature of the rest of civil works to be executed preclude the efficient
packaging of services to be bid, because they will be carried out in several towns across the states.
Under these circumstances, it is unlikely that foreign firms will be interested in participating in the
bidding and thus, many works will be built by local contractors. The large number of experienced
Brazilian contractors guarantees a meaningful competition. Foreign firms will be allowed to participate
in LCB. LCB procedures utilized by the companies have been reviewed by the Bank and found
acceptable. Standard procurement documents (including Letter of Invitation and Draft Contract for
hiring consultants) were jointly prepared by the companies and submitted to the Bank. Project costs
by procurement method are presented in Table 4.1.

l' Consulting services.
124.  Consultants for detailed engineering and training, project supervision in each company, and the
various studies and technical assistance planned under the institutional strengthening component, will
be selected in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for the Use of Consultants (August 1981).



- 33 -
125.  All procurement under ICB will be subject to the Bank's prior review of advertising, bidding
documents, bid evaluation, and contracts awards. In the interest of expediting project execution, the
balance will be subject to ex-post review by sampling. However, in order to ensure that adequate
procurement procedures are followed, the Bank will review and approve--irrespective of the s: :,ount--
the first two contracts under LCB for goods and for works, respectively, of each company. If the Bank
determines in the review by sampling that procurement is inconsistent with agreed procedures, no
expenditures for such items will be financed out of the proceeds of the Loan and the Bank may cancel
an equivalent amount of the Loan.
H. Disbursement
126.  The loan will be disbursed as follows: (i) 100% of foreign expenditures for imported equipment
and materials; (ii) 47% of local expenditures for civil works, equipment, and materials; liii) 47% of local
expenditures for services of consultants domiciled in Brazil and 100% of foreign expenditures for
services of foreign consultants; (iv) 100% of expenditures for all consultant services and training under
the Project's institutional strengthening component; and {v) 100% of foreign expenditures, 100% of
local expenditures Cex-factory cost), and 85% of local expenditures for other items procured locally.
To expedite project execution, two Special Accounts in the amounts of US$14 million and US$1 million
respectively, will be established in the Banco Central do Brasil and in a commercial bank abroad,
acceptable to the Bank, to cover local and foreign currency expenditures, respectively.  Loan
disbursements against civil works contracts below the equivalent of US$3 million and for goods
equivalent to US$250 thousand will be made under the Statements of Expenditure procedure prepared
and certified by SNS/PMU, and CASAN, EMBASA, or SANESUL. Detailed documentation will be
retained by the companies and SNS for inspection during supervision missioris and for audit by the
external auditors of MAS and the SWCs. No force account works are envisaged. The estimated
schedule of disbursement provides for loan disbursement in six years (see Annex 23). The Closing
Date will be December 31, 1997.
I. Advanced Contracting and Retroactive Financing
127.  The Bank loan will finance works to be carried out during the period 1992-1997. Some of the
works are urgently needed--for instance to rehabilitate and expand systems and to restart unaccounted
water control programs--and these are scheduled to start early in 1992. Other works are parts of
annual investment programs with expenditures spread over the agreed investment period. In view of
these needs, the Project will provide for the following: {i) advanced contracting as of January 1, 1992,
and amounting to US$35 million; and (ii) retroactive financing as of the same date, and amounting to
US$15 million. Advance contracting and retroactive financing will be subject to the following
conditions: (i) the SWC should be eligible for project financing at the actionl time; (ii) the subproject
should comp;y with the agreed eligibility criteria; and (iii) the Bank procurement guidelines should have
been followed.
J. Auditing Arrangements
128.  Prior to Loan Negotiations, EMBASA furnished to the Bank an independent auditor's report on
its internal accounting systems and procedures, as requested by the Bank. Recommendations from
this report have been incorporated into the company's institutional development program, to strengthen
its accounting department. During Loan Negotiations, agreement was reached that external auditors
will carry out an audit on the following: li) project accounts; liii financial statements of CASAN,
EMBASA, and SANESUL; Ciii) Statement of Expenditures; and (iv) the Special Account. It was also
agreed at Loan Negotiations that, beginning in 1993, audit reports corresponding to each year will be
submitted to the Bank no later than June 30 of the following year.



- 34 -
V. AGRFEMENTS AND RECOMMENDATIONS
129.  Prior to Loan Negotiations:
(a)  the states and/or the companies furnished to DTN financial counter-guarantees, including
legal authorizations from the State Assemblies (para. 115);
(b)  the state of Bahia and EMBASA agreed on a satisfactory strategy to reduce the
company's debt burden (para. 88).  Copies of the agreements and related legal
documents were furnished to the Bank prior to Loan Negotiations;
(c)  EMBASA furnished to the Bank an independent auditor's report on its internal accounting
systems and procedures, as requested by the Bank. Recommendations from this report
have been incorporated into the company's institutional development program, to
strengthen its accounting department (paras. 60 and 128);
(d)  the companies furnished to the Bank standard procurement documents (para. I 1 9); and
{e)  SNS, the states, and the companies jointly furnished to the Bank drafts of Letters of
Agreement accorded among them (para. 40).
130.  During Loan Negotiations, the Federal Government agreed on:
(a)  TOR satisfactory to the Bank for the creation and staffing of the PMU, including
qualifications and experience of the unit manager (para. 1 13);
(b)  the employment throughout the life of the Project of consultants (TAs) to assist in the
supervision of each of the SWCs, selected based on TOR satisfactory to the Bank, not
later than September 30, 1992 (para. 1 14);
(c)  carrying out the studies and training programs included in the institutional strengthening
component in accordance with terms of reference and timetables satisfactory to the
Bank (para. 43);
(d)  the initial distribution of the Bank loan among CASAN, EMBASA, SANESUL, and
MAS/SNS for the first two years of project execution (para. 45). After this period, Bank
funds will be freely allocated to eligible subprojects provided that the companies meet
technical and financial capability criteria;
(e)  company eligibility criteria for continual participation ir the Project (para. 108);
(f)  authorizing any action required on its part to enable the companies to meet agreed
operational and financial indicators (Annex 12);
(g)  on-lending the proceeds of the loan and providing advances to the companies as
envisaged in the financial plan of the Project (para. I117);
(h)  holding a mid-term review (about June, 1994) to assess completion of studies and
degree of implementation of proposed recommendations; to evaluate progress made by
the SWCs in carrying out plans to improve their commercial performance; to assess the
effectiveness of the training and institutional strengthening to be provided by SNS to the
environmental agencies of Bahia and Mato Grosso do Sul; and to verify compliance with
covenants of the Loan and Project Agreements (para. 120);



- 35 -
{i)   providing project preparation facilities for water demand management and systems
rehab;litation under terms of reference and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para. 43);
(j)   providing training and institutional strengthening to the environmental agencies of Bahia
and Mato Grosso do Sul, in particular on water and wastes related issues (para. 99);
(k)  submiksion to the Bank of progress reports by January 15, April 15, July 15, and
October 15 of each year (para. 121);
(i)  taking any required administrative and legal actions to support the companies to adjust
both current and due accounts cumulatively for inflation, and to eliminate overdue bills
(para. 61);
(m)  the external audit of project accounts, the financial statements of the SWCs, statement
of expenditures, and the Special Account, and its submission to the Bank not later than
June 30 of the following year (para. 128); and
(n)  submission to the Bank of an analysis of key issues related to the Project, and a
retrospective assessment of project implementation from its own perspective, not later
than six months after the loan closing date (para. 121).
131.  During Loan Negotiations, the states of Bahia, Mato Grosso do Sul, and Santa Catarina agreed:
(a)  to authorize, permit, or promote any improvement to the companies' management and
operational systems, and if necessary, any adjustment in the companies' tariff rates to
assure a permanent compliance with the financial performance established in the NTL
and the generation of adequate internal cash consistent with financial plans for the
Project (paras. 61 and 93);
lb)  to assure the timely provision of counterpart financing (para. 84);
{c)  to take any required administrative and legal actions to support the companies to adjust
both current and due accounts cumulatively for inflation, and to eliminate overdue bills
(para. 61); and
(d)  the states of Bahia and Mato Grosso do Sul, in particular, to strengthen their agencies
in charge of reviewing the RIMAS and environmental supervision of water and sewerage
services, through their active participation in the technical assistance program to be
organized by SNS (para. 99).
132.  During Loan Negotiations, CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL agreed:
(a)  to select all individual subprojects, including those outside the Bank-financed part of the
companies' investment programs, based on the following criteria: (i) the subproject
represents the least-cost alternative; and (ii) the subproject's LRAIC does not exceed the
current average tariff in more than a preestablished percentage (paras. 109 to 112);
lb)  to comply with the financial performance established in the NTL (para. 93);
(c)  to take all measures necessary to generate sufficient internal cash to annually cover an
increasing percentage of their total new investment costs--excluding interest during
construction, beginning with at least 20% in fiscal year 1992, reaching at least 24% in
fiscal year 1994, and at least 35% in fiscal year 1997 and thereafter (para. 93);



- 36 -
(d)  to refrain from changing annual investments during the project implementation period by
more than 20% of the investment amounts originally agreed, without prior Bank approval
(para. 73);
(e)  to gradually reduce personnel costs by: (i) improving labor productivity, as measured by
the number of water and sewerage connections per employee, so that by December,
1993, it should have been increased to at least 195 in CASAN, 175 in EMBASA, and
245 in SANESUL, and by December, 1997, to at least 225 in CASAN, 210 in EMBASA,
and 260 in SANESUL (para. 58); and (ii) diminishing personnel costs as a percentage of
operating costs--excluding depreciation, so that by December, 1993, it should have been
decreased to at most 75% in CASAN, 66% in EMBASA, and 64% in SANESUL, and by
December, 1997, to at most 74% in CASAN, 63% in EMBASA, and 63% in SANESUL
(para. 59);
(f)  to achieve a debt service coverage ratio no lower than 1.5 (para. 93);
(g)  to carry out programs satisfactory to the Bank to improve their commercial performance
vis-a-vis agreed operating and financial targets (para. 93 and Annex 12);
(h)  to adjust overdue accounts for inflation or take other measures which would reduce the
financial losses resulting from overdue bills (para. 61);
(i)  to hold two workshops on project implementation, one not later than two months after
Loan Effectiveness and the second during implementation but before end-1994 (para.
117);
(j)  to submit to the Bank, not later than October 15 of each year, a general financial
projection for the following six-year period, with specific details on investment plans for
the upcoming year (para. 85); and
1k)  to prepare detailed studies on: (i) tariffs before December 31, 1993, based on marginal
costs calculated to show compliance with subprojects eligibility criteria, discuss the
results and agree with the Bank on a set of recommendations, and implement them
before June 30, 1994 (para. 82); (ii) the amounts involved and likely fiscal implications
of an asset revaluation before June 30, 1993, and incorporate the results into the
audited financial statements of fiscal year 1993 (para. 91). These studies should be
repeated every three years to determine the necessity of further physical revaluations;
and (iii) SANESUL, in particular, a detailed study of the state's hydro-geological potential
before December 31, 1993, and based on its conclusions and recommendations, review
the economic justification of water production subprojects involving superficial water
(para. 49).
133.  Prior to Loan Effectiveness:
(a)  at least two of the companies, should have signed on-lending contracts with the Federal
Government. The remaining on-lending contract, if any, would become a condition for
disbursement for the respective company (para. 115); and
(b)  Letters of Agreement among the SWCs, the state governments, and SNS satisfactory
to the Bank, should be signed and submitted to the Bank (para. 11 7).
134.  Subject to the above conditions, the Project is siuitable for a Bank loan of US$250 million
equivalent, to be repaid over a period of 15 years--including a five-year grace period, at the Bank's
standard variable rate. Advanced contracting for up to US$35 million and retroactive financing for up
to US$10 million for expenditures incurred after January 1, 1992, is also recommended (para. 127).
