BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Brazilian Water Supply and Sewerage Tariffs
General Pricing Criteria
1.    Brazilian State Water Supply and Sewerage Companies (SWCs) apply a uniform tariff structure
known as the National Tariff Law (NTL). The NTL was established by Law No. 6,528 in May 11,
1978, and further delimited by Decree No. 82,587 in November 6, 1978. The NTL was conceived by
the National Water Supply and Sanitation Program (PLANASA) as the major underpinning framework
of sector regulation and implemented under the direction of the former Banco Nacional da Habitagao
(BNH).
2.    The NTL seeks financial equilibrium for the SWCs and social equity for the customers. Financial
equilibrium is sought by requiring the SWCs to have average tariffs for water supply and sewerage
services high enough for their revenues to meet all their financial expenses, including an appropriate
remuneration for capital. Social equity is sought by requiring the SWCs to consider the customer's
ability to pay when setting tariffs.
Financial Guidelines
3.     Financial equilibrium is guaranteed by requiring each utility to have an average tariff -1 for
water supply and sewerage service such that it should generate a revenue equal to its service cost
(SC). SC is defined as the sum of operating expenses--personnel, materials, electricity and other third
party services, general, and fiscal expenses (DEX); annual allowances for depreciation, doubtful
accounts, and amortization of expenses (DPA); and a remuneration for investment (RI) that should be
at least equal to the debt service--interest payments and capital amortization due in a given period of
time, but not larger than 12% of the acknowledged investment in operating fixed assets.
Social Equity Guidelines
4.    The current social guidelines on water supply and sewerage tariffs require utilities to take into
account users' ability to pay. Rate structures are defined using minimum consumption levels and
increasing block tariffs. Assumptions underlying this requirement are: (i) there are households which
are classified as commercial or industrial customers, and lii) family income is positively correlated with
family water consumption. In addition, the current guidelines explicitly deny subsidies to commercial
and industrial customers, and suggest a neutral tariff (a tariff equal to the company's service cost) for
public users. Social equity guidelines are discussed in detail in the following paragraphs.
Minimum Residential Bill
5.    The first guideline establishes that there should be a minimum water residential bill not
exceeding 0.35 OTN (Obrigacoes do Tesouro Nacional), and a minimum water and sewerage residential
bill not exceeding 0.50 OTN. In 1983, the average minimum water supply and sewerage residential
bill reached 70% of the maximum value authorized by the law. By 1986, this figure was reduced to
less than 60%, and the average minimum water supply residential bill was near 50% of the maximum
limit. The abolition of the OTN in 1990 has turned this guideline into an useless parameter.
31/ Average tariffs are defined dividing operational revenue, y volume sold. Required average tariffs are defined dividing service
cost by volume sold.



- 44 -
ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 7
Minimum Residential Consumption Level
6.    A  second guideline establishes that: (i) the minimum  residential consumption level
corresponding to the minimum bill should be at least equal to 103 per household per month, and (ii) this
volume should represent the quantity of water a family requires for maintaining minimum and
indispensable health and sanitary conditions. In 1988, 18 SWCs had their minimum consumption level
(MCL) at 10 m3/household-month (hm), one had it at 12 m3/hm, and 2 at 20 m3/hm.  The
concentration at 10 m31hm is a relatively recent trend.
7.    Establishing a MCL is equivalent to setting a fixed rate with a right to consume the minimum
level free of charge. Usually, the main reason for including MCLs in a rate schedule is that it allows
utilities a full or partial recovery of customer-related fixed costs. Establishing MCLs for accommodating
low-income households--as it seems to be happening in the Brazilian case--does not generally
accomplish its purpose; on the contrary, it contributes to significant departures from the objective of
improving poor families' welfare when handled aggressively.
8.    In addition, it is not only the problem of having some low-consumption customers paying
average tariffs greater than high-consumption users. It may be the case that many residential,
commercial, industrial, and public customers who are able to afford the utilities' service cost (without
violating NTL's directives) are being subsidized.
Residential Consumption Blocks
9.    A third guideline establishes that residential consumption should be differentiated by increasing
block tariffs. The blocks' ranges vary significantly not only from one utility to another, but also
between areas being served by the same utility (for example, CAERN has three different schedules).
In addition, the formation of the blocks does not follow any specific guideline. For exan.ple, the range
of the second block goes from 11 m3/hm to 15 m3/hm in 9 SWCs, from 11 m3/hm to 20 m3/hm in 6
SWCs, from 16 m3/hm to 30 m3/hm in 3 SWCs, and exhibits eight different ranges in the remaining
12 SWCs.
Residential Water Consumption and Family Income
10.   Current social guidelines on water supply and sewerage tariffs are based on the assumption
that family levels of income and water consumption are positively correlated. Minimum bills, minimum
consumption levels, and principally, consumption blocks, are elements of the rate schedule because
of this assumption. However, if this assumption is proven inaccurate, the entire apparatus involving
social considerations could be regressive and some households could be paying bills smaller than what
they can afford. In addition, a large majority of households are subsidized when residential rates are
smaller than marginal costs--as is Brazil's most common case. This fact gives utilities a large room for
increasing revenues just by eliminating unnecessary subsidies now being granted to high-income
families.
11.   A 1987 study by ACCORSI & ASSOCIADOS examined this assumption and concluded that in
Brazil "water consumption is not an appropriate variable for the purpose of guaranteeing progressive
tariffs, because of its weak relationship with family income." 21 The studs found a correlation
coefficient between these two variables of only 0.21.
12.   Table 1 shows average household water consumption by income level in SANEPAR (Parana's
SWC). The small differences among average values of the first four categories, as well as the large
2I ACCORSI & ASSOCIADOS LTDA. O1987). Avaliaco dos Normas e Critsrios de Fixacao de Terifas no Setor do Soneamanto:
Uma AnMliso dos Obietivos de Equidede, 2 Volumenes. Curitiba, Agosto. SANEPAR - CEF (BNH), p.138.

standard deviation exhibited by all income categories, are worth noting. Household water consumption
distribution for each income category is shown in Table 2 and illustrated in Figure 1. The small range
defined between 7 m3/hm and 1 5 m3/hm includes almost half of SANEPAR residential consumers.
13.   The use of water consumption as a main element in water supply pricing may result in tariff
structures that are not as progressive as originally planned and, in some cases, even in regressive tariff
structures. This issue has been pointed out by several authors. For example, CHAVES DE AZEVEDO
and BAZANELLA stated that 2.: "...the majority of the tariff structures of the Companhias de
Saneamento present unsatisfactory progressive levels which cause even inverse transfers of subsidies."
However, it would be wrong to argue that the solution is to make the tariff structure more progressive,
sharply increasing the rates belonging to higher consumption blocks. This proposal could make the
tariff structure appear progressive for the average poor family. But upon closer examination, it will
surely aggravate the situation of many households--individually considered.

Residential Water Consumption and Other Variables
14.   In the study mentioned above, residential water consumption (RWC) could not be successfully
explained by other variables. Geographical location and urban size failed to explain the differences
between water consumption of households located in different cities. An explanation of RWC using
variables such as: number of water exits (points) (W), number of people living in each house (P),
number of rooms (R), the area of each house (A), and the type of construction (C), also failed when
applied to SANEPAR's case.

Non-residential Tariffs
1 5.   A fourth guideline establishes that tariffs should be differentiated by final usage, and that
average commercial and industrial rates should be greater than the utility's average rate.
16.   A fifth guideline establishes that: {i) commercial, industrial, and public 4' minimum
consumption levels should also be set, and (ii) in each category, the minimum consumption rate should
be smaller than the remaining rates, in that category. Additionally, commercial and industrial minimum
rates should be greater than the utility's average tariff, and the public minimum rate should be greater
than the residential minimum rate.
17.   Differentiating between commercial, industrial, or public customers based on their water
consumption level has negative effects on the resource allocation process. Brazilians seem to be aware
of this point, as minimum consumption levels for non-residential customers are explained by some
households owning a small family business, and in turn, being classified as commercial or industrial
customers.  However, a well designed tariff structure should automatically take care of this
classification process. Households having small family business within or attached to their homes
should find themselves better off when classified as residential customers. Likewise, households
having a larger than normal family business should find themselves better off when classified as non-
domestic customers.
Non-metered Consumption
18.   In 1988, twelve (12) SWCs charged non-metered residential customers with only their
authorized minimum bills. The remaining companies used their rate schedules, and water consumption
estimates based on the following criteria: dwellings' area in m2 (5), dwelling construction type (3),
number of rooms (2), number of water exits (1), or a combination of more than one of the above (1).
4/ The public category also includes some private entties involved in social activitias.

19.   Most non-residential water consumption is metered, and those customers that are not metered
are generally non-intensive water users. SWCs use one of the following criteria to estimate water
consumption: establishment are in m2, economic activity, minimum consumption, number of water
exits, or a combination of more than one of the above criteria.
Special Contracts
20.   Special contracts are celebrated between non-residential customers and eight of the SWCs.
These contracts usually guarantee a minimum amount of water to be supplied to the customer
benefitting from such contract. In this case, the customer negotiates his rates aside from the actual
rate schedule. The water volume sold under this kind of agreement represents a significant share for
some of these companies, regardless of the small number of customers involved.
Sewerage Rates
21.   Sewerage and water supply tariff structures are the same, with sewerage rates oscillating
between 35% and 100% of water rates. In 1988, there were 15 SWCs applying a 100% overcharge,
9 applying 80%, 2 applying 60%, 2 applying 50%, while the remaining three applied 90%, 75%, and
35%, respectively.
22.   Sewerage rates are not based on supply costs, but on financial reasons. Explicit charges for
treatment do not exist.  Systems without treatment have rates significantly larger than costs,
meanwhile systems with treatment have costs much greater than rates. Therefore, sewerage systems
are usually transferring a large portion of its revenue to water supply systems, given that in most cases
only a small portion (if at all) of the collected sewage is treated.
Complementary Guidelines
23.   A long term objective of each utility is to serve the entire population under its area of
responsibility. Therefore, as a way to facilitate access to their services, most utilities do not charge
connection fees to their customers or, if they do, it only covers materials, labor, and the meter cost,
if applicable.
24.   SWCs usually have several water supply and sewerage systems with different supply costs
which are aggregated and consolidated for financial and pricing purposes. Therefore, SWCs' rate
schedules and levels are uniformly applied to all customers, independent of the individual system that
serves them. This pricing feature introduces a cross-subsidization element among customers of a given
utility. Funhermore, differences in the interest rate PLANASA applies to the different SWCs introduces
another cross-subsidization element between states.
Efficient Allocation of Resources
25.   Water supply and sewerage pricing criteria are complemented by planning criteria that aim to
obtain an efficient allocation of resources. Under PLANASA, utilities are required to expand their
systems choosing the minimum cost alternativeE, and water projects are accepted only if the utility's
water average tariff (WAT) Is larger than 0.75 of the project's marginal cost (MC), for cities with
populations larger than 50,000 people. The WAT should be larger than 0.60 MC for localities with
populations between 5,000 and 50,000 people. Sewerage projects are accepted only if the utility's
sewerage average tariff is at least equal to 0.60 MC. However, a project that does not satisfy these

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Investment Programs for 1992-1997: CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL
1.    The six year investment program  (1992-1997) of CASAN  (Attachment 1), EMBASA
(Attachment 4), and SANESUL (Attachment 7) have been conceived to increase water and sewerage
service levels and the operational efficiency of the companies. At the end of the implementation
period, about 3.34 million people will have gained access to water supply and more than 0.94 million
people to water-borne sewerage service. After 1997, a significant number of additional people will
also benefit from subprojects that, although being the least-cost solution, will be exhibiting varVing
degrees of idle capacity during the first operational years. Other important physical objectives are
included with the agreed monitoring indicators presented in Annex 12.
A. CASAN
2.    As shown in Attachment 1, CASAN will invest a total of US$336.6 million. The capital region
(Florian6poiis, San Jose, Palhoca e Santo Amaro da Emperatriz)--which accounts for 21% of that urban
population in the state, will receive US$80.0 million 124%) of the planned investments; Jcoinville,
Crici(ima, and other medium-sized cities (population between 50,000 to 500,000) will receive
US$160.7 million (48%); small-sized towns (population between 5,000 and 50,000) US$47.2 mii'ion
114%); and rural communities (population below 5,000) US$33.5 million 010%). Investments in wtAter
supply and sewerage will account for 50% and 46%, respectively, of the total, while US$15.1 naillion
(5%) will be allocated to instituitional development programs.
3.    The main components of CASAN's investment program are:
a.    Water Supply
The expansion of CASAN's water supply systems seeks to increase thO region's coverago level
from the current 85% to 93% in 1997. The following works will be executed to serve an
additional population of about 1 .0 million people:
1)    Florian6polis Integrated System
Water Production. Water intake capacity will bo increased by about 0.40 m3/s--principally
through the addition of new wells to the svstems, andt treatment capacity by about 0.52 ml/s.
Pumping Stations. The nominal capacity of tho pumping stations will bo Incroased by about
0.40 m3/s.
Transmission Pipelines. TI-he total length of the transmission network will be increased by
about 37 km. The availability of raw water will be expanded by building 13 km of transmission
pipelines to the water treatmient plants anrd the reliability of the delivery system will be
improved by buildingI 24 km of pipelines from thio water treatment plants to tlle storage tanks.
Storage Tanks. Storage capacity will be increased by about 11,600 n13.
Distribution System. About 568 km of primary and secondary distribution pipes and 44,900
connections will be installcd.

Rehabilitation and Other Programs. Small investment programs throughout several of CASAN's
water supply systems will also be made to improve its reliability, remove bottlenecks and
reduce operating costs.
2)    Other Medium-Sized Cities {population between 50,000 and 500,000)
Water Production. Water intake capacity will be increased by about 2.07 m3/s and treatment
capacity by about 2.52 m3/s.
Transmission Pipelines. The total length of the transmission network will be increased by
about 135 km. The availability of saw water will be expanded by building 40 km of
transmission pipelines to the water treatment plants and the reliability of the delivery system
will be improved by building 95 km of pipelines from the water treatment plants to the storage
tanks.
Pumping Stations. The nominal capacity of the pumping stations will be increased by about
4.17 m3/s.
Storage Tanks. Storage capacity will be increased by about 57,450 m3.
Distribution System. About 1,077 km of primary an'd secondary distribution pipes and 78,802
connections will be installed.
Rehabilitatioin and Other Programs. Small investment programs throughout several of CASAN's
water supply systems will also be made to improve its reliability, remove bottlenecks and
reduce operating costs.
3)    Small-Sized Cities and Towns (population between 5,000 and 50,000)
Water Production. Water intake capacitV will be increased by about 1.45 m3/s--principally
through the addition of new wells to the systems, and treatment capacity by about 1.49 m3/s.
Transmission Pipelines. The total length of the transmission network will be increased by

Rehabilitation and Other Programs. Small investment programs throughout several of CASAN's

4)    Rural Communities (population below 5,000)

customers. Theso programs entail construction of some 35,700 house connections.

b.    Sewerage
CASAN's expansion of the sewerage systems will increase its coverage level from the current
9% to 21 % in 1997. An additional population of about 0.5 million people will be attended.
A very important fact of this sewerage investment program is that all projects include final
treatment and disposal. Works to be executed are:
1)    Florian6polis Integrated System
Interceptors, Treatment and Final Disposal. To increase the volume of sewage collected that
is treated, about 9.1 km of large diameter interceptors will be constructed, the nominal
capacity of the stabilization ponds will be increased in about 0.68 m3/s, and about 7.0 km of
comp,lementary pipes for final disposition will be built.
Pumping Stations. The nominal capacity of pumping stations will be increased by about 0.98
m3/s.
Collection Network. About 2.8 km of main collectors, 241.0 km of secondary collectors, and
22,200 house discharges will be added to these sewerage systems.
Rehabilitation and Other Programs. Small investment programs will be made throughout these
sewerage systems to improve service, remove bottlenecks ar.n reduce operating costs.
2)    Other Medium-Sized Cities (population between 50,000 and 500,000)
Interceptors, Treatment and Final Disposal. To increase the volume of sewage collected that
is treated, about 9.7 km of large diameter interceptors will be constructed, the nominal
capacity of the stabilization ponds will be increased in about 0.56 m3/s, and at-out 4.6 km of
complementary pipes for final disposition will be built.
Pumping Stations. The nominal capacity of pumping stations will be increased by about 1.35
m3/s.
Collection Network. About 38.9 km of main collectors, 507.0 km of secondary collectors, and
48,400 house discharges will be added to these sewerage systems.
Rehabilitation and Other Programs. Small investment programs will be made throughout these
sewerage systems to improve service, remove bottlenecks and reduce operating costs.
3)    Small-Sized Cities and Towns (population between 5,000 and 50,00(i)
Interceptors, Treatment and Final Disposal. To increase the volume of sewage collected that
is treated, the nominal capacity of the stabilization ponds will be increased in about 0.25 m3/s
and about 2.8 km of complementary pipes for final disposition will be built.
Pumping Stations. The nominal capacity of pumping stations will be increased by about 0.60
m31s.
Collection Network. About 7.7 km of main collectors, 73.0 km of secondary collectors, and
4,500 house discharges will be added to these sewerage systems.
Rehabilitation and Other Programs. Small investment programs will be made throughout these
sewerage systems to improve service, remove bottlenecks and reduce operating costs.

c.    Operational Development
Technical assistance, materials and works for: (i) macrometering and pitometry, to improve
reliability and coverage of production metering, and to improve pressure zoning, ascertain pipe
roughness coefficients and delivery flows; (ii) micrometering, to develop maintenance and
replacement programs for meters, and to acquire and install about 36,000 new meters; (iii)
technical cadastre, to carry out a physical inventory of about 2,400 km of networks; (iv) user's
cadastre, to assess and redesign the basic classification of about 120,000 connections; and
(v) planning and operational control, to acquire and install 30 new radio-communications and
automatic systems in four operating units.
d.    Managerial Development
Restructuration of CASAN's area of data processing, including acquisition of a new computer
and development of new management systems, to reduce billing time and improve collection
efficiency.  Renovation of its transportation vehicles to improve CASAN's response to
consumer complaints. Improvement of its inventories' techniques. Implementation of a quality
control program. And construction and equipment of a training center.
e.    Projects
Because CASAN already has many basic projects concluded, most projects will consist in
reassessments, technical assistance, and prepar3tion of executive projects.
4.    The Bank project (see Attachment 2) would finance about 48% of CASAN's tota' investment
program. Subprojects excleded from the Bank proect are (see Attachments 2 and 3): {i) all sewerage
works being financed by IDB and the Federal Government under the PROSEGE program; (ii) all water
and sewerage works already with financing contracts signed with CEF; (iii) all sewerage works planned
in the cities of Videira, CriciOma, e Capador, and complementary sewerage works in Florian6polis; liv)
expansion of those water supply systems for which alternative sources of financing are already
available; (v) all water supply expansion programs in rural communities; and (vi) the acquisition of
veNicles and computers under the institutional development program.
D. EMBASA
5.    As shown in Attachment 4, EMBASA will invest a total of US$385.6 million. *rhe Metropolitan
Region of Salvador--which accounts for 41 % of tho urban population in the stato, will receive
US$178.6 million (46%) of the planned investments; medium-sized cities will receivo US$75.7 million
(20%); small-sized towns US$44.0 million (1 1 /); and rural communities US$20.0 million (5%).
Investments in water supply and sewerage will account for 64% and 19%, respectively, of the total,
while US$54.1 million (14%) will be allocated to institutional development programs and US$13.3
million (3%) to prepare projects and buy general goods.
6.    Tho main components of EMBASA's investment program are:
a.    Water Supply
The expansion of EMBASA's water supply system seeks to increaso the region's coverage level
froim the current 72%X to 80% in 1997. Tho following vvorks will ho executed to serve an
additional population of about 1,718 million peoplo:

1 >    Metropolitan Region of Salvador

Transmission Pipelines. Construction of 23 km of transmission lines to improve the reliability

Distribution System. About 64 km of primary and 360 km of secondary distribution pipes, and

4)    Rural Communities (population below 5,000)
Investment programs in about 40 water supply systems to accommodate demands from new

b.    Sewerage
1)    Metropolitan Region of Salvador
The expansion of the sewerage system of Salvador's Metropolitan Region will increase the
urban region's coverage level to 23% in 1 997--from its current level of 17%, benefiting the
region as follows: (i} about 5 km of seashore will be recovered by discharging about 4 m3Is of
sewage through an existing submarine outfall; (ii) a very strong septic sewage odor currently
present from the collection point until the ocean will be eliminated; (iii) the existing outfall will
be fully utilized. Built in 1975 to attend a population of about 1.6 million persons (with a cost
of about US$30 million), the outfall is currently used by less than 0.25 million persons; (iv)
scme urban beaches of Todos os Santos Bay will be recovered for bathing usage; and (v) most
open ocean beaches will be recovered by collecting the dry season discharge of the
Camarugipe River (only two small streams with reasonable water quality would keep on
discharging at the seashore). To accomplish these objectives, the following works will be
executed:
Interceptors and Treatment. Construction of a main pipeline of about 4 km at the final portion
of tho drainage basin to collect the dry season discharge of the Camarugipe River and send it
to the ocean. Network implementation of three small drainage basins along the main river,
close to the diversion point.  This inclusion would add 165,000 persons to the system.
Construction of about 3 km of interceptors in Itapua's area. These works will increase the
average utilization of the existing submarino pipeline to 5.4 m3/s_ its nominal capacity is 7.8
m3/s and its current average utilization is about 1.4 m3/s.
Collection Network. About 200 km of collectors and 30,000 house discharges will be added
to the sewerage system.
c.    Operational Development
The objectives of this program are to: (i) reduce unaccounted-for-water from 44.7% (the level
observed In 1990) to 33.3% in 1996; (ii) reduce operational costs; tiii) increase billing and
collecting; (iv) increase EMBASA's productivity; (v) seek economies of scale; (vi) test new
technologies; and (vii) institutionalize operational planning in the company. To reach these
objectives, EMBASA's operational development program requires techinical assistance,
materials arid works for the followinU subprojccts: (i) macrometening, to improve reliability of
production metering by increasing its coverage from 70% to 100%/o in 1996; (ii) pitometry, to
improve pressure zoning, ascertain pipe roughness ;oefficients and delivery flows by creating
and training 30 pitomoetic teams and implemeniting 65 pitometric districts; {iii) micrometering,
to increase the percentage of water connections metered to about 76% in 1997 (from its
current level of 54%') and to develop maintenance and replacement programs for those meters;

ANNEX 14
Page 7 of 9
(iv) consumer services (SIP-SAP), to improve EMBASA's productivity and response to
consumer complaints through training, the replacement of part of its vehicles, better data
systematization, and a program of minor reparations; (v} technical cadastre, to carry out a
physical inventory of about 2,400 km of networks to attain the 50% mark; (vi) maintenance
and rehabilitation of operational units, to develop and execute maintenance and rehabilitation
programs in different operational units, including its etectro-mechanic and electrical equipment;
{vii) planning and operational control, to acquire and install equipment for better water quality
control, implement new operational control activities, and review its technical and operational
manuals; (viii) user's cadastre, to assess and redesign the users' basic classification; and (ix)
billing and collection, to improve EMBASA's organizational methods to reduce billing time and
improve collection efficiency.
d.    Managerial Development
Restructuration of EMBASA's area of data processing, including the acquisition of new
equipment and technical assistance and training.
7.    The Bank project, presented in Attachment 5, would finance about 52% of Eiv1BASA's total
investment program. Subprojects excluded from the Bank project are (see Attachments 6 and 7): (i)
all water and sewerage works with financing contracts already signed; (ii) water distribution pipes and
connections and sewerage collectors and connections planned to be built in Salvador's Metropolitan
Regioni from 1995 to 1997; (iii) the water investment program in all medium-sized cities with the
exception of Vitoria da Conquista, Feira de Santana e Jequie--the three largest cities; (iv) all water
supply expansion programs in small-sized cities and towns and ru,al communities; (v) the entire
managerial development program and the acquisition of vehicles under the operational development
program; and (vi) future project preparation and acquisition of general goods.
C. SANESUL
8.    As shown in Attachment 7, SANESUL will invest a total of US$215.1 million. Its capital
Campo Grande--which accounts for 39% of the urbarn population in the state, will receive US$111.3
million (52%) of the planned investments; medium-sized cities will receive US$19.3 million (9%); small-
sized towns US$54.8 million (25%); and rural communities US$2.4 million 1 %). Investments in water
supply and sewerage will account for 71% and 22%, respectively, of the total, while US$10.6 million
(15%) will be used for preparing new projects and US$ 16.7 million (8%) will be allocated to
institutional development programs.
9.    The main components of SANESUL's investment program are:
a.    Water Supply
The expansion of SANESUL's water supply systems will increase its coverage level from the
current 83% to 96% in 1997. The following works will be executed to serve an additional
population of about 630 thousand people (about 71,500 house connections will be built by
developers during the planning period 1992-1997):
1)    Campo Grande
Projected works will increase water coverage lovel to 96% in 1997 (from 83% in 1991) and
allow future demand to be attended until year 2000.
Water Production. Perforation of 11 wells with a total projected capacity of about 0.22 m3 s
to increase the total capacitV of the Guariroba system to about 0.67 m3fs. And construction



- 75 -
ANNEX 14
Page 8 of 9
of 2 pumping stations and 16.0 km of water transmission pipes to increase raw water
availability in the treatment plants.
Transmission Pipelines. Construction of about 26 km of transmission lines to improve the
reliability of the delivery system from the water treatment plants to the storage tanks.
Storage Tanks and Pumping Stations. Construction of 4 new stations to pump treated water
to the distribution system. And construction of 10 storage tanks to increase storage capacitY
by about 26,000 m3.
Distribution System. About 244 km of primary and 382 km of secondary distribution pipes and
about 25,000 connections will be installed.
2)    Medium-Sized Cities (population between 50,000 and 500,000)
Projected works will increase 1997 water coverage level to 95% in Dourados, Corumba-
Ladario, and Tres Lagoas from the current 1991 levels of 72%, 90%, and 90%, respectively.
These works will also allow future demand in these cities to be attended until year 2000.
Water Production. Water intake and treatment capacity will be increased by about 330 It/s
(230 I/s in Corumba-Ladario and 100 I/s in Tres Lagoas, where 3 wells will be drilled). About
8.5 km of transmission pipes and 4 pumping stations for raw water will be built .
Transmission Pipelines. Construction of about 6 km of transmission pipes in Corumba-Ladario
to improve the reliability of the delivery system from the water treatment plants to the storage
tanks.
Storage Tanks. One storage tank will be built in Corumba-Ladario to increase storage capacity
by 1,000 m3.
Distribution System. About 35 km of primary and 203 km of secondary distribution pipes and
9,300 connections will be installed.
3)    Water Supply In Small-Sized Cities and Towns (population between 5,000 and 50.000)
Projected works will allow to maintain current water coverage level in 47 cities and towns.
Water Production. Water intake and treatment capacity will be increased by about 170 It/s.
and about 2.5 km of transmission pipes for raw water will be added to these systems.
Transmission Pipelines. Construction of about 8.4 km of transmission pipes to improve the
reliability of the delivery system from the water treatment plants to tho storage tanks.
Storage Tanks. Storage capacity will be increased by about 15,400 m3.
Distribution System. About 29 km of primary and 414 km of secondary distribution pipes, and
22,600 connections will be installed.
4)    Rural Communities (rIopulation below 5,000)
Investment programs in several water supply systems to accommodate demands from new
customers. These programs entail construction of some 8,078 house connections.



- 76 -
ANNEX 14
Page 9 of 9
b.    Sewerage
The expansion of SANESUL's sewerage systems will increase its coverage level from the
current 11 % to 17% in 1997. An additional population of about 170 thousand people will be
attended. Subprojects to be executed are under preparation for the following areas:
1)    Campo Grande
The few urban areas that hzvp, current access to sewerage systems use the Prosa and Segredo
Rivers as their final destination points without any kind of treatment. Sewage produced in
these regions will now be collected and pumped for aorated ponds and then discharged into
the rivers. In other areas that are not currently served by the sewerage system--low-income
districts, in general, a septic tank scheme will be implemented with a program of settled sludge
cleaning and treatment at the lagoons. This solution will improve the ecological conditions of
the rivers, and the risk of general waterborne diseases contamination during the rainy season
flooding will be reduced.
2)    Cities and Towns of the Interior
Low-cost technology projects based on septic tanks and anaerobic filters, for example, will be
prepared and implemented at localities until 50,000 inhabitants.
C.    Institutional Development
Programs on operational and managerial development aim at reducing unaccounted-for water
to 30% in 1997 (from its current level of about 36%), and operating costs, and improving
water and sewerage systems reliability, billing and collection efficiency, and consumer services.
In particular, technical assistance, materials and works will be required for: {i) macrometering
and pitometry, to improve reliability and coverage of production metering, and to improve
pressure zoning, ascertain pipe roughness coefficients and delivery flows; (ii) micrometering,
to implement maintenance and replacement programs for meters; {iii) technical cadastre, to
continue with a physical inventory of the water distribution network; (iv) user's cadastre, to
assess and redesign the users' basic classification; {v) operational units, to develop and
execute maintenance and rehabilitation programs in different operational units throughout
several of SANESUL's water supply and sewerage systems, to improve their reliability, remove
bottlenecks and reduce operating costs; (vi) planning and operational control, to acquire and
Install equipment for better water quality control, implement new operational control activities,
and review its technical and operational manuals; Ivii) consumer services, to improve
SANESUL's response to consumer complaints; (viii) billing and collection, to restructure
SANESUL's area of data processing, optimize organizational methods, reduce billing time and
improve collection efficiency; and (ix) technical assistance and training.
d.    Projects
Most projects will include technical assistance for the preparation of basic and executive
projects.
10.   The Bank project, presented in Attachment 8, would finance about 60% of SANESUL's total
investment program. Subprojects excluded from the Bank project are (see Attachments 8 and 9): (i)
all sewerage works being financed by IDB and the Federal Government under the PROSEGE program;
ii) all water and sewerage works already with financing contracts signed with CEF; (iii) expansion of
Campo Grarnde's sewerage system; (iv) the aistribution part of the water investment program in half
of targeted small-sized towns; {v) all water supply expansion programs in rural communities; and (vi)
the acquisition of vehicles and computers under the institutional development program.

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Methodology and Results of the Marginal Cost Pricing Analysis
1.     Marginal cost pricing analysis has been applied to evaluate the tariff structures and the
economic benefits and cost-effectiveness of prospective water and sewerage investments in the states
of Bahia, Mato Grosso do Sul, and Santa Catarina. Long-run average incremental costs (LRAIC) have
been estimated for eight water supply systems and one sewage collection and treatment system
managed by the SWCs of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL. The planning horizon covers 30 years
(1992-2021) and the opportunity cost of capital (OCC) is assumed to be 10%.
Projections of Water Productioni and Consumption
2.     Urban population, projected by the compaiiies based on census data, is assumed to growth at
a uniform rate from 1991 to the year in which the expanded water supply system saturates. A star
is attached to the year in which the extended production capacity of the water supply system is
saturated, namely, when water production minus unaccounted-for water is equal or smaller than water
consumption. Urban population served is simply the product of total urban population and the
estimated service coverage level. Urban population is assumed to be static after the saturation year,
constraint by the project's water supply capacity. The number of households served is calculated
dividing the urban population served by the estimated number of persons per household, which is
assumed constant at their 1990 levels throughout the entire planning horizon. The incremental number
of households served in year t is calculated as the difference between the number of households
served in year t and the number of households served in year (t-1). Assuming implicitly that water
demand is inelastic to price changes, total water consumption is projected as the product of the urban
population served by an estimated daily water consumption per capita. The average unaccounted-for
water level is assumed to decrease during project implementation as a result of the actions to be taken
under the institutional development program, and then to remain steady at its 1997 level.'. Water
production is computed as the sum of water consumed and lost in the transmission anc distribution
networkz'. The total incremental water production is calculated as the difference between the current
water production level and the water production level in the base year (i.e., 1991). The production
capacity of the water supply system is expanded when the project becomes operational. The index
of excess capacity shows the percentage of the current production capacity which is not utilized. The
total incremental benefit resulting from new investments is divided into the benefit from reducing
unaccounted-for water, the benefit from restoring supply reliability, and the benefit from expanding
supply capacity. The benefit from reducing unaccounted-for water, BRL, is equal to the additional
water available to old and new customers resulting from a company's accumulative efforts to decrease
11 The assumption of constant unaccounted-for water levels after project completion, that is, after 1997, requires from the
companies to completely internalized into their day-to-day operations all the measures that allowed them to reach their new
levels. In the case used to illustrate this methodology, the costs associated with keeping all those measures in placed once
project execution Is completed are implicitly assumed to be part of thq companios' total operating costs. However, an explicit
recognition of these costs will improve the cost-benefit analysis of reducing unaccounted-for water levels and produce a more
reliable estimate of the LRAIC of this type of subprojects.

its average loss i b:e-. The benefit from restoring supply reliability, BRR,, results from a partial or total
elimination of an overloading operation of treatment plants4'. The benefit from expanding supply
capacity, BEC,, is equal to the nominal capacity of additional facilities built under the projectr-'.
However, given the V'vct that the benefit from expanding supply capacity is usually fulIV captured only
after several years of the beginning of project operation, an additional element to adjust total benefit
needs to be introduced. This adjustment benefit, BEX, is by definition!' usually negative during the
first years of project oper.iion. The net present value of the incremental amount of water to be
supplied by the project is then calculated using the assumed OCC. The discounted present values of
benefits from reducing unaccounted-for water, restoring supply reliability, expanding supply capacity,
and the adjustment benefit are calculated analogously. As an example, projections on water
consumption and production in Florian6polis (CASAN) are presented ir, Attachment 1.
Projection of Sewage Collection and Treatment
3.     Urban population served with sewerage connections is estimated as the product of total urban
population and projected service coverage levels. The projected amount of sewage collected in each
year is estimated based on the number of sewerage connections projected for each year and the
amount of sewage produced per connection in each year. The amount of sewage produced per
connection may vary annually (in Florianapolis, for example, where the number of households per
connection is assumed to decrease during the project implementation period, the amount of sewage
produced per connection also decreases during the entire implementation period). The incremental
amount of sewage collected in year t is calculated as the difference between sewage collected in year
t and in the base year. The total and incremental amounts of sewage collected and treated are
calculated analogously. The net present value of the incremental amounts of sewage collected and
sewage collected and treated by the project are then calculated using the assumed OCC.
S/ The following formula is used to approximately calculate the marginal benefit from reducing unaccounted-for water:

Incremental Costs in Water Supply Systems
4.    Incremental costs in water supply systems result from building new capacity, reducing
unaccounted-for water levels, and operating and maintaining new facilities. The capacity cost includes
investments for water intake, water supply treatment, transmission, primary distribution, secondary
distribution, and connections. Complementary investments--mainly house connections and secondary
distribution water pipes--to be built after project execution is completed and until the year in which the
system capacity is saturated are also included. Complementary investment costs are calculated based
on the number of incremental households to be served in each year and an assumed cost per
household of building the required facilities. The investment cost for reducing unaccounted-for water
levels corresponds to a fraction of the total cost of the companies' institutional development program.
All investment costs are expressed in market prices of June, 1991, and include engineering and
physical contingencies. Since fixed operating and maintenance (O&M) costs are associated with the
formed capital stock, they should be accumulated over years. Incremental fixed O&M costs in year
t are then assumed to be proportional to capacity investment costs accumulated from year 1 to year
t. Total investment costs in each year are equal to the sum of capacity investment costs plus the cost
of reducing unaccounted-for water plus incremental fixed O&M costs in the same year. The net
present value of these stream of costs are calculated using the assumed OCC as the discount rate.
As an example, projected investment and O&M costs for Florian6polis (CASAN) are presented in
Attachment 2.
Incremental Costs in Sewerage Systems
5.    Incremental costs in sewerage systems result from building new capacity and operating and
maintaining new facilities.  The capacity cost includes investments for sewerage connections,
secondary collectors, main collectors and pumping stations, interceptors, and final disposition and
treatment. Complementary investments--mainly house connections and secondary collectors--to be
built after project execution is completed are also included. Complementary investment costs are
calculated based on the number of incremental households to be served in each year and an assumed
cost per household of building the required facilities. As in water supply, all investment costs are
expressed in market prices of June, 1991, and include engineering and physical contingencies. Also
as in water supply, fixed operating and maintenance (O&M) costs are associated with the formed
capital stock and are accumulated over years. Incremental fixed O&M costs in year t are then assumed
to be proportional to capacity investment costs accumulated from year I to year t. Again as in water,
the net present value of all streams of costs are calculated using the assumed 0CC as the discount
rate.
Calculation of Long-Run Average Incremental Costs
6.    Long-run average incremental costs (LRAIC) have been calculated at each stage of the water
supply chain as the ratio of the sum of the present value of the respective capacity investment and
incremental fixed O&M costs to the discounted present amount of incremental water production. Since
investments that reduce unaccounted-for water levels produce results that are equivalent to increasing
water production capacity, the present value of these investments should be added to the present
value of increasing water intake. Since LRAIC are calculated based on incremental water production,
an adjustment for water losses should be made at each stage of the water supply system to find the
actual marginal cost. To this end and based on the current unaccounted-for water distribution, loss

investment and incremental fixed O&M costs to the discounted present amount of incremental sewage
collection (connections, collectors, and main collectors) or incremenzal sewage collected and treated
(interceptors and final disposition and treatment).
Financial and Economic Subsidies
8.    The tariff structures of CASAN, EMBASA, and SANESUL are shown in Annex 1 7. Financial
subsidies (or overcharges) are calculated, in each system and for each tariff category, by comparing
the average tariff rate charged to that specific group of customers with the company's overall average
tariff rate. Economic subsides (or overcharges) are calculated, in each system and for each tariff
category, by comparing the average tariff rate charged to that specific group of customers with the
marginal cost to provide the service to them. As an exarple, estimated financial and economic
subsidies for Salvador (EMBASA), Florian6polis (CASAN) are shown in Attachment 4, and for Campo
Grande, Corumba/Ladario, Tres Lagoas, Miranda, Sete Quedas, and Guia Lopes (SANESUL) are shown
in Attachment 5.

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Notes and Assumptions for Financial Forecasts
A. Introduction
1.    The financial projections are based on: la) audited financial statements of CASAN, SANESUL,
and EMBASA for FY 1986-1990; (b) FY 1991 budget; and (c) financial projections and explanatory
notes for 1992-1996, as prepared by the companies.
2.     Because of the high inflation in Brazil during the past decade, historical financial statements
have been translated into current US$ (using year-end exchange rates for balance sheets and average
exchange rates for income statements), and projections have also been prepared in current US$. The
coefficients used for projected international inflation (source: IEIRD OD 6.50, Annex B, May 1991),
historic domestic inflation and exchange rates (source: Conjuntura EconOmica, March 1991) are shown
below:
End-year      Average        Domestic      International
Exch.Rate     Exch.Rate      Inflation     Inflat.lndex
Year           Cr$/US$       Cr$/US$        ./M)           1990=100
1986M            0.0151        0.0137            65            80.7
1987             0.0727        0.0394           416            88.6
1988             0.7878        0.2628         1,038            95.1
19897'          11.8465        2.8408         1,783            94.4
1990w'         170.3500       68.5500         1,477           100.0
1991                                                          103.9
1992                                                          107.9
1993                                                          112.0
1994                                                          116.4
1995                                                          120.9
1996                                                          125.5
1997                                                          130.4
B. Income Statement
Revenues
3.     Operating revenues are derived from the sale of water and sewage collection and treatment
services. Consumers, volumes, and revenues are broken down into two major regions: capital and
Interior. Projections assume that water volumes billed per customer will remain essentially the same
during the projection period. Other operating revenues, which include sale of equipment and consumer
connection, installation and service fees, and penalties for delayed payments, are derived as a given
lti On February 28. 1986, as part of the Cruzado Plan, the oruzado (Cz$M replaced the cruzeiro at the rate of 1 = 1,000.
21  On January 15. 1989, as part of the Summer Plan, the cruzado novo (NCz$) replaced the cruzado at the rate of 1 = 1,000.
31 In March 6, 1990, as part of the Collor Plan, the government renamed the currency the cruzeiro (Cr6).



- 108 -
ANNEX 19
Page 2 of 3
percentage of the water and sewerage services sold (7.5% in CASAN, 7.0% in EMBASA, and 4.0%
In SANESUL).
4.    Retail prices. Financial performance improved dramatically from 1987 to 1990, because of
large real increases in the average tariff levels during these years, as shown in Annex 18. Tariff levels
are projected in US$ prices. Estimated 1991 average tariff levels are based on 1991 projected average
tariff levels, and projected average tariff levels for the remaining years are based on a percentage of
their projected values at December, 1991, as follows: CASAN, 90% in 1992, 92% in 1993, 94% in
1994, 96% in 1995, 98% in 1996, and 100% in 1997; EMBASA, 100% in 1992, 104% in 1993,
106% in 1994, 107% in 1995, 108% in 1996, and 109% in 1997; and SANESUL, 104% in 1992,
108% in 1993, 110% in 1994, 112% in 1995, 113% in 1996, and 117% in 1997. These levels
enable the companies to comply with the conditionality of the loan.
5.    Unaccounted-for water is measured by the difference between water produced and water
consumed, and include losses due to meter errors, theft and other items. Forecasts assume CASAN,
EMBASA, and SANESUL meet targeted unaccounted-for water levels by the end of the project period.
(See Monitoring Indicators, Annex 17.)
Expenses
6.    Operating expenses include personnel, materials (chemicals and others), electricity, other third
party services, general, and fiscal costs. Variable costs (chemicals and electricity) are projected based
on the companies' 1991 budgeted unitary estimates and their annual projected production volumes.
Personnel costs are projected reflecting annual productivity gains of 3.6%  in CASAN, 5.1%  in
EMBASA, and 2.0% in SANESUL (see Monitoring Indicators, Annex 12), combined with annual real
increases in the average salary of 0.5% in CASAN, 1.1 % in EMBASA and 0.6% in SANESUL. Fixed
costs are assumed to increase in accordance with the increase in the number of both water and
sewerage connections.
7.    Depreciation is calculated on a straight-line basis taken into account the assets expected useful
life. The effect of proposed asset revaluation studies (see para. 91) was not taken into account,
although they will surely recommend to significantly increase the value of the operating assets (in the
SWC of Sao Paulo, for example, a similar study resulted in a recommendation to multiply by 4.58 times
the book values of the reviewed fixed operating assets). Provision for doubtful accounts is based on
about 3% of accounts receivabie.
8.    Net financial income and expenses is the net of interest on long-term debt (excluding interest
during construction on the IBRD, CEF and FAE loans, which is capitalized) and interest income from
short-term investments.
9.    Total inflation adjustment, in the historic financial statements, is the net of monetary
corrections on asset and equity accounts, and monetary variations on long and short term debt.
10.   Income tax for the water sector was increased from its historical 6% level (compared with 35%
private corporate income tax) to 30% during FY 1989. Brazilian corporate law allows for an income
tax loss carry-forward of up to four years, thus the companies assumed no provision for income tax.
C. Balance Sheet
Assets
11.   Accounts receivable are assumed to decrease to 45 days of yearly sales revenue by 1993 in
CASAN, and to 45 days by 1997 in EMBASA and SANESUL. Accounts receivable consist of accounts
billed and unpaid.



- 109 -                              ANNEX 19
Page 3 of 3
12.   Inventory consists of materials and, according to the company, represents between 40 and 60
days of supplies.
Liabilities and Equity
13.   Long-term debt consists of debt belonging to loans contracted with IBRD, IDB, CEF, FAE or
others that has more than 360 days before becoming exigible. EMBASA's long-term deb-; has been
reduced following the agreement reached with the State of Bahia. The agreement specifies that the
State will convert Into shares EMBASA's debt with the FAE ai id pay EMBASA's debt service with CEF.
14.   Share capital is assumed to incr ise with the increased equity participation of the state over
the period, as shown in the financing plan (see Funds Flow, Attachments 3, 6, and 9).
D. Borrowing and Investments
Project Financing
15.   Proposed IBRD Loan: CASAN, US$79.2 million equivalent borrowed over 15 years, including
5 years of grace, at the Bank's variable lending rate--currently 7.73% (source: IBRD OD 3.10, Annex
A, July 1991). EMBASA, US$99.4 million equivalent borrowed over 15 years, including 5 years of
grace, et the variable lending rate. SANESUL, US$63.4 million equivalent borrowed over 15 years,
including 5 years of grace, at the variable lending rate. In addition, the MAS/SNS will receive US$8.0
million equivalent borrowed over 15 years, including 5 years of grace, at the variable lending rate. First
disbursements are expected during mid-1 992, and first repayments during mid-1 997. Commitment
fee: 0.75% of undisbursed balance.
Non-Project Investment Borrowing
16.   PLANASA: CASAN, US$23.1 million equivalent from CEF and US$28.5 million equivalent from
FAE borrowed over 25 years, including 2 years of grace, at 2.5% (FAE) and 12.0% (CEF). EMBASA
contemplates no new borrowing from other sources. SANESUL, US$14.6 million equivalent from CEF
and US$34.6 million equivalent from FAE borrowed over 25 years, including 2 years of grace, at 2.5%
(FAE) and 6.0% (CEF). Forecasts of FAEs resources for the companies' investment programs were
based solely on the company's estimated repayments to the FAEs for the period.
Total Investment Costs
17.   Total investment costs are projected to include interest during construction on the IBRD, CEF,
and FAE loans, and administrative costs of about 10% of total investments, which are capitalized
(representing physical improvements and other items allowed under Brazilian accounting standards).
These items are separated from physical investments.

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Sample of a Letter of Agreement
CASAN, the State of Santa Catarina and the SNS
Objectives of the Agreement
1.    The purpose of this agreement is to clarify the respective responsibilities of CASAN, the State
of Santa Catarina, and the National Secretariat for Water Supply and Sanitation (SNS) in relation to the
provision of water and sanitation services in the state of Santa Catarina during the period 1992-1997,
to establish a set of guidelines for appraising their performance, and to improve their mutual
understanding regarding accountability in the respective roles. The rationale for this agreement arises
from the mutual interest of CASAN, the State of Santa Catarina, and the SNS:
* to support greater decentralization and competition in the water supply and sanitation sector;
*  to increase the quantity and quality of water and sanixatiorn services in the state of Santa
Catarina;
* to optimize investment and financial planning and project execution to reach, within the agreed
limits, the physical goals established by the Federal and State govarnments for expansion of
the water supply and sanitation sector;
* to set and maintain water and sewerage tariffs at levels which allow CASAN greater financial
autonomy and the implementation of its 1992-1997 investment program--within expected
terms; and
i to promote an improvement of CASAN's operating and financial performance.
Period of Agreement
2.    This agreement covers the period between its date of signature and December 31, 1997,
coinciding with the implementation period of CASAN's investment progrim--estimated 2t about
US$337 million. However, the period of this agreement may be extended at an', time with the consent
of the signatories.
Commitments and Responsibilities of CASAN
3.    CASAN agrees to take all measures necessary to improve its standards of commercial
performance and, in particular:
* to implement its 1992-1997 investment program with efficiency and efficacy, and within
expected terms, to achieve the water supply and sewerage service levels shown in Annex A
and the financial results shown in Annex B;
* to submit to the state and the SNS, not latter than September 30 of each year, an updated
general financial projection for the following six-year period--with specific details on investment
plans for the upcoming year, based on the Globai Feasibility Simulation Model (SIVIG) or in a
similar one to be agreed with the SNS;



- 112 -
ANNEX 21
Page 2 of 3
* to gradually improve its standards of operating and financial performance to reach, within
expected terms, the monitoring indicators shown in Annex C;
* to implement and start using by June 30, 1992, ar. information system to be agreed with the
state and the SNS;
* to take all actions necessary on revenue generation and cost control to assure permanent
compliance with the National Tariff Law (NTL) and the generation of adequate internal cash
consistent with financial plans and monitoring indicators shown in Annexes B and C,
respectively;
* to prepare a new tariff proposal by July 31, 1994, based on a detailed marginal cost pricing
study, including a timetable for implementation;
* to prepare a detailed study on the amounts involved and likely fiscal implications of an asset
revaluation, and incorporate the results into the audited financial statements of fiscal year
1993. This study should be repeated every three years to determine the necessity of further
physical revaltiations;
* to adjust overdue accounts for inflation or take other measures which would reduce the
financial losses resulting from overdue bills; and
: to prepare a detailed study on water demand management and systems rehabilitation before
July 31, 1994, including a proposal for its implementation.
Commitments and Responsibilities of the State of Santa Catarina
4.    The State of Santa Catarina agrees to take all measures necessary to help improve CASAN's
standards of commercial performance and, in particular:
* to restraint from curtailing CASAN's administrative and financial autonomy;
- to authorize or not to take any action that would prevent periodic increases in CASAN's
nominal tariffs to assure permanent compliance with the NTL and the generation of adequate
internal cash consistent with financial plans shown in Annex B;
* to assure the timely provision of the counterpart financing shown in Annex B; and
* to take any required administrative and legal actions to support CASAN's efforts to adjust both
current and due accounts cumulatively for inflation, and to eliminate overdue bills.
Commitments and Responsibilities of the SNS
5.    The SNS agrees to take all measures necessary to help improve CASAN's standards of
commercial performance and, in particular:
* to restraint from curtailing CASAN's administrative and financial autonomy;
* to authorize or not to take any action that would prevent periodic increases in CASAN's
nominal tariffs to assure permanent compliance with the NTL and the generation of adequate
internal cash consistent with financial plans shown in Annex B;
* to support CASAN's efforts to obtain new financial resources In either the private or public
capital markets; and



113 -
ANNEX 21
Page 3 of 3
0 to take any required administrative and legal actions to support CASAN's efforts to adjust both
current and due accounts cumulatively for inflation, and to eliminate overdue bills.
Evaluation. Compliance, and Publication
6.    All three parties are responsible for compliance with this Agreement, as well as with the
permanent evaluation of advances made in the water and wastes sector in the State of Santa Catarina.
This Agreement will be published in the Federal and state Official Diaries. The SNS will pay the fees
corresponding to publication in the Federal Official Diary and the State the fees corresponding to
publication in the State Official Diary.
Annexes:

B. Financing Plan
C. Monitoring Indicators

BRAZIL
WATER SECTOR MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Guidelines for Evaluation of Subprojects
Background
1.    These guidelines have been prepared: (i) to help design subprojects that contribute towards
optimizing company allocation of scarce resources; {ii) to permit the financial and institutional
strengthening of the SWCs; (iii) to improve environmental and health conditions in the SWCs' areas
of responsibility; and (iv) to provide a common framework for subproject evaluation by all parties
Involved In this operation: SNS, the state governments, the SWCs, the Project Management Unit
(PMU), the Technical Agents (TA), and the Bank.
2.    Each proposed subproject should correspond to the least-cost solution, and should be the
object of a feasibility study prepared in accordance with these guidelines. All feasibility studies should
be prepared by the SWCs, reviewed by the TA, and approved by the PMU.
3.    A subproject is the sum of one or more components from three main categories: (a) institutional
development programs; (b) rehabilitation of existing infrastructure, and (c) construction of new
facilities. These categories are listed in descending order or priority. The composition of a subproject,
however, should be tailored to the specific needs of a given locality.
4.    The analysis of a given subproject seeks to establish the priority of the proposed components,
its consistency with sector policies, and its contribution to improve the delivery of services in a given
locality. The analysis also aims to ascertain that neither the subproject nor Its components adversely
affect the environment and that their benefits equal or exceed their costs.
5.    Seve;al steps should be followed to perform this analysis in a systematic and effective way.
It is first necessary to describe and evaluate the current water supply and/or sewerage systems, as
well as assess both the existing and future demands for these services. Second, feasible alternatives
to remove institutional and physical constraints are explored and compared, leading to the selection
of subproject components. The end result is an investment and action plan designed to improve the
performance of the company and its capacity to meet future demand at minimum cost. Third, an
environmental assessment is done to ensure that the proposed works meet acceptable environmental
protection standards. Fourth, a marginal cost pricing analysis is preformed to evaluate the tariff
structure, the economic benefits, and the cost-effectiveness of prospective water and sewerage
investments.
Institutional Development Programs
6.    The SWCs' programs directed towards improving their efficiency in delivering water and
sewerage services have been justified on the basis of a general assessment of their overall design and
main organizational units, of the effectiveness of the proposed financial, commercial, and operational
activities, and of the training needs of their staff. To assure an accurate post-evaluation of these
programs, the SWCs should develop appropriate accounting systems to carefully record all costs and
benefits associated with them. The level of aggregation will depend on the nature of the activities--for
example, costs and benefits associated with financial improvements may be aggregated in a single
account, but not costs and benefits associated with operational Improvements.



- 115 -                                ANNEX-22
Page 2 of 5
Rehabilitation Program
7.    The evaluation of a water supply and sewerage system begins with an engineering assessment
of the main existing components of the system (main physical dimension, materials, cond!tlon,
reliability, and capacity). Major components include:
(a)   Water Supply:
Intake facilities
Transmisslon pipelines
Treatment plants
Storage tanks
Primary distribution network
Pumping stations
Secondary distribution network
Connections
(b)  Sewerage System:
Connections
Collectors network
Main collectors
Interceptors
Puniping stations
Outfalls
Treatment plants
8.    This engineering evaluation provides the basis for assessing the feasibility of rehabilitating the
system's components to bring them up to their optimal capacity at a cost usually lower than that
required to construct a new facility.
Demand Analysis
9.    The evaluation of a water supply or sewerage system continues with an analysis of existing
coverage levels and type of service provided as well as per capita and aggregate consumption and
production volume. The demand analysis should treat the following aspects in detail:
(a)  existing coverage levels and type of service provided (total population, population served,
number of house connections, standpipes, septic tanks, etc.);
(b)  water metered consumption (based on house connections), preferably broken down by main
users (residential, commercial, industrial, and public) and estimated unmetered consumption
(including a detailed explanation and justification of the criteria utilized to estimate it);
(c)  water metered production and estimated unmetered production (with the same breakdown and
explanations as (b) above); and
(d)  estimates of unaccounted-for water (UFW). UFW  is defined as the difference between
production and metered consumption.
10.   The analysis of historical production, UFW, and demand should cover the previous two to five
years. Production, UFW, and demand projections should be based on the projected growth of house
connections or service accounts and consider the effects of metering consumption, plans to reduce
UFW and improve commercialization practices, and possible price elasticity effects. Projections should



- 116 -                               ANIEgX 22
Page 3 of 5
be made on a yearly basis up to the system's time of saturation. Sanitary sewerage flow demand
estimates should also be made on the basis of the number of connections to the sewer system and
reasonable assumptions on consumption and Infiltration flows. Sewage collection projections should
bW broken down by type of final disposition used.
Optimal Expansion of System's Components
11.   In determining the optimum investment expansion plan li.e., the least-cost solution), the
following aspects should be taken into account:
(a)  all investment works should be built or expanded as late as possible, as determined by the
demand projection and the existing capacity of the component under analysis; and
(b)  facilities should be sized so as to minimize idle capacity. Several models are available to
determine the optimum size and sequencing of investments.
12.   One model often used to determine the optimum size of water and sewerage works is based
on the observation that these works present economies of scale and assumes a linear demand growth
(a conservative assumption). Based on a discount rate of 10% and average economy of scale factors,
the following design periods are recommended:
Intake works                     20 years
Deep wells                       10 years
Transmission pipelines           15 years
Distribution system              15 years
Water treatment plants           10 years
Storage tanks                    10 years
Pumping stations (civil works)   20 years
Equipment                        10 years
Meters                            5 years
Sewerage pipe systems            20 years
Sewage treatment plants          10 years
13.   Design periods greater than those indicated or for components not shown should be justified
by a detailed analysis. Financial constraints may justify the use of shorter design periods.
Analysis and Selection of Alternatives
14.   To determine the optimal expansion program of a water supply or sewerage system, an analysis
of all technically feasible alternatives should be made and the most promising ones, particularly
rehabilitation works, compared on the basis of discounted total costs (capital and operational) and on
a present value or equivalent annual basis. The one with the lowest cost should be selected. The
discount rate to use is 10%. A sensitivity analysis is recommended when alternatives present similar
value or annual costs, this can help in the selection of the best alternative.
15.   It should be remembered that only economic costs enter into the calculations. Costs should
be expressed in constant prices; that is, inflation should not be taken into account. Financial and
transfer costs such as interest, depreciation, and taxes should not enter into the calculations.
Moreover, economic costs to be included are the incremental investment and operational costs
associated with the component. These costs are determined on the basis of an analysis of the
situation "with" and "without" the project, which is not necessarily the same as "before" and "after"
the project. Annex 16 contains a detailed description of the methodology to be used for calculating
water and sewerage incremental costs.



- 117 -                               ANNEX 22
Page 4 of 5
16.   For all main investment components included In a subloan, it is necessary to develop unit costs
and tangible monitoring indicators that can be easily followed during project execution. These
indicators should be routinely used during project execution as control devices to ensure that all
investments will produce the intended results and remain cost effective. Unit cost indicators are also
intended to create a cost data bank to be used to improve cost estimates for future subprojects and
to help identify cost-effective and replicable programs.
17.   Benefit and cost parameters should be followed closely during project construction. An adverse
change In any of these parameters should trigger a review of the project and, if necessary, the
subproJect or component should be modified to ensure that maximum net benefits continue to accrue.
Environmental Assessment
1 8.   All subprojects will be screened to determine the degree, if any, to which potential
environmental impacts may occur. Also considered will be any proposed actions, that will prevent or
mitigate any adverse effects and ensure that the subproject is environmentally sound and sustainable.
Many subprojects are likely to require only a limited environmental analysis. Each assessment ', ill
include:
(a)   a detailed description of the project and of relevant environmental conditions;
(b)  an analysis of positive and negative impacts likely to result from the project's components;
{c)  an analysis of alternatives to mitigate any adverse effects and costs and benefits of proposed
remedial actions;
{d)  a proposed plan to reduce potentially significant adverse environmental impacts to acceptable
levels, its costs (capital and recurrent) and institutional and training requirements, if any; and
(e)   a monitoring plan regarding environmental impacts and performance.
Project Justification
19.   Each individual subproject should be economically justified using marginal costs calculated on
the basis of the incremental demand it will satisfy, the estimated investment cost--including
complementary works, and the estimated operational and maintenance cost Csee Annex 16). In large
cities--more than 500 thousand inhabitants, water projects will be approved only if the company's
current average water tariff in that city (AWTi) exceeds 75% of the marginal cost of providing the
water supply service to residential customers with the given project CMCWi) in 1992, 80% in 1993,
and 85% in 1994 and afterwards. In medium cities--more than 50 thousand inhabitants but less or
equal than 500 thousand, water projects will be approved onlv if the AWTi exceeds 75% of the MCWi
in 1992 and 80% in 1993 and afterwards. In small cities and towns--more than 5 thousand
inhabitants but less or equal than 50 thousand, water projects will be approved only if the AWTi
exceeds 60% of the MCWi in 1992, 65% in 1993, and 70% in 1994 and afterwards. In rural
communities--population less or equal than 5 thousand, water projects will be approved only if the
AWTi exceeds 60% of the MCWi.
20.   Sewerage projects in large cities will be approved only if the company's current average
sewerage tariff in the project's site CASTi) exceeds 60% of the marginal cost of providing the sewerage
service to residential customers with the given project (MCSi) in 1992, 70% in 1993; 80% in 1994;
and 85% in 1995 anid afterwNards. Sewerage projects in medium cities will be approved only if the
ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi in 1992, 70% in 1993, and 80% in 1994 and afterwards. Sewerage
projects in small cities and towns will be approved only if the ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi in 1992,



- 118 -                                 NNEX 2
Page 5 of 5
65% In 1993, and 70% in 1994 and afterwards. Sewerage projects in rural communities will be
approved only if the ASTi exceeds 60% of the MCSi.
21.   Projects not satisfying the above conditions but which are designed for minimum cost may be
accepted by the Bank on a case-by-case basis. The analysis should explicitly address the following
issues: (i) the lack of alternative and feasible solutions; (ii) the Impossibility of recovering costs through
tariffs; and (iii) the impact on poverty alleviation, and environmental and health conditions. These
subprojects are likely to be in localities where urban poverty prevails and large investments are needed
to compensate for the lack of previous investments.
